Refuting the Claim That God Created the World for a Cause
Baqillani refutes that God created for a cause via a disjunctive argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that God's creation of the world cannot be for a cause, whether eternal or originated, because any such cause would entail logical absurdities.

Core Idea
God's acts are not caused by need; any cause would compromise His eternity.

Baqillani tackles a subtle objection: if God created for a cause, that cause must be either eternal or originated. In this passage, he refutes the first possibility, showing that an eternal cause would make the world eternal, which contradicts its createdness. He begins to address the second, but the argument is cut short. Nevertheless, the logic is clear: God's actions are free and uncaused.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial answer
Start by reading the opening objection: 'Do you say that the Eternal created the world for a cause?' and Baqillani's immediate answer: 'No, because causes are not permissible for Him.' Notice how he dismisses the idea first on the grounds that causes are about benefit and harm, which don't apply to God.
This gives a quick theological principle: God is above need.
2
Follow the disjunctive argument (eternal cause)
Baqillani then presents a stronger argument: if there were a cause, it would be either eternal or originated. Focus on the eternal case—he argues that an eternal cause would necessitate the world to be eternal, or if there's a temporal delay, it would make the eternal cause become originated. That's impossible, so the eternal cause is invalid.
This is the core of the lesson: demonstrating the contradiction.
3
Reflect on why the argument is incomplete
Notice the text cuts off at the start of discussing the second possibility (originated cause). Even though we don't have the full argument, you can infer that an originated cause would need another cause, leading to infinite regress. Reflect on how the partial argument still serves to show the impossibility of a cause for creation.
This highlights the overall lesson: God's creation is uncaused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في أن القديم لم يفعل العالم لعلة
فإن قال قائل: فهل تقولون إن القديم فعل العالم لعلة أوجبت حدوثه منه؟ قيل له : لا لأن العلل لا تجوز عليه لأنها مقصورة على جرّ المنافع ودفع المضارّ ويدل على ذلك أيضاً أنه لو كان تعالى فاعلاً للعالم لعلة أوجبته لم تخل تلك العلة أن تكون قديمة أو مُحدّثة، فإن كانت قديمةً من وجب قدمُ العالمَ لِقِدَم علّته وألا يكون بين العلة القديمة وبين وجود العالم إلا مقدار زمان الإيجاد وذلك يوجب حدوث القديم لأن ما لم يكن قبل المُحْدَث إلا بزمان أو أزمنة محدودة وجب حدوثه لأن فائدة توقيت وجود الشيء هو أنه كان معدوماً قبل تلك الحال، فلما لم يَجُز حدوث القديم لم يَجُز أن يكون العالم مُحْدَثاً لعلة قديمة، وإن كانت تلك العلة محدثة فلا يخلو
Issue: That the Eternal did not create the world for a cause.
If someone says: Do you say that the Eternal created the world for a cause that necessitated its origination from Him? It is said to him: No, because causes are not permissible for Him, since they are confined to drawing benefits and repelling harms. This is also indicated by the fact that if He, exalted, were the agent of the world for a cause that necessitated it, that cause would have to be either eternal (qadīma) or originated (muḥdatha). If it were eternal, then the eternity of the world would be necessitated due to the eternity of its cause, and there would be between the eternal cause and the existence of the world only the duration of the act of bringing-into-existence (zamān al-ījād), and that necessitates the originatedness of the eternal, because that which did not exist before the originated except for a limited duration or durations is necessitated to be originated, since the benefit of timing the existence of a thing is that it was non-existent before that state. So when the originatedness of the eternal is not permissible, it is not permissible that the world be originated from an eternal cause. And if that cause were originated, then it cannot but [the text ends here]
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument that God did not create the world for a cause ('illa). Baqillani first states the answer: no, because causes are only relevant for drawing benefits and repelling harms, which does not apply to God. He then presents a further argument: if God acted for a cause that necessitated the world's origination, that cause must be either eternal (qadīma) or originated (muḥdatha). If it is eternal, then the world must be eternal as well (because a cause would always have its effect unless there is a temporal delay). But if there is only a temporal delay (the 'duration of bringing-into-existence'), then that would make the eternal cause itself originated, because its effect — the world — would be time-bound. Since the Eternal cannot be originated, an eternal cause is impossible. The passage cuts off before completing the second possibility (an originated cause), but the implication is that an originated cause would require another cause ad infinitum, which is absurd. Thus, no cause for creation is permissible.

Key Terms
علة
قديم
مُحدَث
إيجاد
علة قديمة
علة محدثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God created the world for an eternal cause (e.g., a timeless reason) is acceptable.
Correction: Baqillani shows that an eternal cause would entail the world being eternal or the cause becoming temporal—both contradictions. Thus, an eternal cause is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the problem with the idea that God created the world for an eternal cause?
A) An eternal cause would make the world eternal, which contradicts its originatedness.
B) An eternal cause would need to be created itself.
C) An eternal cause would limit God's power.
D) An eternal cause is impossible because God has no needs.

Key Takeaway
God's creation is not caused by any need; any alleged cause would undermine His transcendence and lead to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument says causes are for drawing benefits and repelling harms. In what subtle ways do you attribute human-like motives to God, expecting Him to act according to your sense of fairness or benefit? How will you silence that expectation and trust that His actions are beyond any cause you could comprehend?

The Wise Agent's Action Without Cause
Baqillani responds to an objection about divine action without cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's response to the objection that God must act for a cause, and his refutation based on the difference between God and creatures regarding benefit and harm.

Core Idea
God's actions are not bound by the same causes as human actions.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani addresses a common objection: if we never see a wise human act without a purpose, how can God act without a cause? He first clarifies that human wise agents do act for benefit or harm. But then he explains that the analogy fails because God is beyond benefit and harm. Finally, he turns the tables by showing that the objectors themselves accept unobservable realities, so their own criterion is inconsistent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the initial question: 'Have you found a wise agent who performs an action without a cause while possessing knowledge and remembrance?' This sets up the challenge.
The objector assumes that observed behavior defines what is possible.
2
Grasp Baqillani's answer
Notice the distinction: human wise agents act for benefit or harm, but God is not subject to such. Therefore the analogy fails.
Baqillani points out that the difference in the possibility of benefit/harm breaks the analogy.
3
See the broader refutation
Baqillani then shows that if only observable realities were accepted, many doctrines held by the objectors themselves would be impossible. This reveals their inconsistency.
The doctrines include events without beginning, eternity of the body, prime matter, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في علة الفعل الصادر عن الفاعل الحكيم. فإن قال قائل : فهل وجدتم فاعلاً حكيماً يفعل الفعل لا لعلة مع العلم والذكر؟ قيل له : لا إذا صح أن يوقعه على وجه يصح انتفاعه به أو دفع الضرّ عنه، فإن قال : فيجب حمل أمر القديم في فعله على حال فاعلنا ؛ قيل له : لا يجب ذلك لافتراقهما في علة جواز النفع والضرر عليهما وقد مرّ ذلك بما يغني عن ردّه . ويقال لهم : لو لم يصح القضاء إلا بما شوهد ووُجد لوجب إحالة ما تذهبون إليه من إثبات حوادث لا أوّل لها وإحالة القضاء على قدم الجسم وأنه لا أول لوجوده وإحالة قول من قال منكم بأن الهيولي والطينة قد من الكمية والصورة والكيفية وجميع الأعراض وإحالة قول من قال كانت خلت
Issue: Regarding the cause of the action issuing from the wise agent. If someone says: 'Have you found a wise agent who performs an action without a cause while possessing knowledge and remembrance?' He is told: 'No, if it is valid that he performs it in a manner that he can benefit from it or repel harm from himself.' If he says: 'Then it is necessary to apply the case of the Eternal in His action to the state of our agent,' he is told: 'That is not necessary because of their difference regarding the possibility of benefit and harm upon them, and that has already been mentioned in a way that suffices from repeating the reply.' And it is said to them: 'If judgment were only valid on the basis of what is witnessed and found, then it would be necessary to deem impossible what you yourselves affirm of establishing events without beginning, and to deem impossible the judgment of the eternity of the body and that it has no beginning of its existence, and to deem impossible the statement of those among you who say that the matter (hayūlā) and clay (ṭīna) have been from quantity, form, quality, and all accidents, and to deem impossible the statement of those who say 'they were created'...'
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage contains a second objection and answer plus a broader refutation. The objector asks whether we have ever observed a wise agent acting without a cause while being knowing and mindful. The answer: No, if the act is done in a way that the agent can benefit from it or repel harm. That is, observed wise agents always act for a purpose related to benefit/harm. The objector then argues that by analogy, God's actions should also have such a cause. Baqillani responds that the analogy fails because humans are subject to benefit and harm, while God is not. This was already explained sufficiently. Then Baqillani turns the tables: if we only accept judgments based on observable experience, then many doctrines held by the objectors (likely the Mu'tazila or others) would be invalid, such as the existence of events without beginning (e.g., the eternity of the world for those who affirm it), the eternity of the body, and the existence of prime matter (hayūlā) and accidents independently. This shows that the objectors' own principles are inconsistent. The passage ends abruptly at 'they were created', indicating the text continues on the next page.

Key Terms
فاعل حكيم (fāʿil ḥakīm)
علة ('illa)
نفع (nafʿ)
ضرر (ḍarar)
حوادث لا أول لها (ḥawādith lā awwala lahā)
قدم الجسم (qidam al-jism)
هيولي (hayūlā)
طينة (ṭīna)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God must act for a cause just like human wise agents because we never observe otherwise.
Correction: Baqillani clarifies that the analogy fails because humans are subject to benefit/harm, but God is not. Moreover, the objectors themselves accept many unobservable realities.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani reject the analogy between God and a human wise agent regarding action without cause?
A) Because no wise agent exists
B) Because God does not act at all
C) Because humans are subject to benefit and harm while God is not
D) Because the objector has not observed enough actions

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the objection that God must act for a cause by showing the difference between God and humans regarding benefit/harm and by exposing the inconsistency of the objectors' own criteria.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficulty in life, do you immediately assume there is no divine wisdom behind it because you cannot see a reason? How does this lesson challenge you to trust God's actions even when they lack apparent cause?
?
Are you tempted to judge God's actions by human standards, expecting Him to act in ways that make sense to your limited perspective? What would it cost you to surrender that expectation and acknowledge His transcendence?
?
If you were to apply the objector's principle—only accepting what is observed—to your own life, what unobserved realities (like the afterlife, angels, or divine decree) would you have to reject? How does that inconsistency compel you to rethink your approach to faith?

The Impossibility of Infinite Causal Regress
Baqillani argues that an infinite regress of causes makes the world's existence impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that an infinite chain of causes makes the world's existence impossible, and why the alternative of uncaused events leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
An infinite regress of causes never arrives at existence.

Baqillani presents a logical dilemma: either every cause requires another cause, leading to an infinite regress, or some causes are uncaused. He shows that both options lead to absurdity, forcing us to accept a first cause with wisdom. This passage is a classic example of reductio ad absurdum in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two-part structure of the argument.
The passage uses technical terms like 'illa (cause) and muhdatha (brought into existence).
2
Understand the first horn: infinite regress
Baqillani argues that if every cause has a cause, we get an infinite chain, which makes the world's existence impossible because it can never be actualized.
This is a classic argument against infinite regress.
3
Understand the second horn: uncaused events
If some causes are uncaused and the agent is wise, then all events could be uncaused, which is absurd because it removes purpose and order.
The key is that a wise agent does not act without reason for every event.
4
Grasp the conclusion: necessity of a first cause
By eliminating both horns, Baqillani forces the conclusion that there must be a first cause that is uncaused but not arbitrary; it must be wise and purposeful.
This leads to the Islamic concept of a Creator.
5
Reflect on the argument's structure
Notice how Baqillani uses a dichotomy and reductio to eliminate alternatives. This method is common in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُحْدِثها أن يكون أحدثها لعلّة أو لا لعلة فإن كانت محدّثة لعلة وعلتها أيضاً مُحْدَثة وجب أن تكون علة العلة مُحْدَثة لعلة أخرى وكذلك أبداً إلى غير غاية وذلك يُحيل وجود العالم جملةً لتعلقه بما يستحيل فعله وخروجه إلى الوجود، وإن كانت العلة والخاطر والداعي والباعث والمحرك محدثةً لا لعلة وكانت بالوجود لما وجدت من فاعلها أولى منها بالعدم لا لعلة . وكان فاعلها حكيماً غير سفيه جاز حدوث سائر الحوادث منه لا لعلة وكان حكيماً غير سفيه ولم يكن خروجه عن السفه بإحداث مُحدث واحد لا لعلة أولى من خروجه عنه بإحداث جميع الحوادث لا لعلة وهذا يبطل ما توهموه إيطالاً ظاهراً .
Its maker [must] be that it brought it into existence with a cause or without a cause. If it is brought into existence with a cause, and its cause is also brought into existence [with a cause], then the cause of the cause must be brought into existence with another cause, and so on forever without limit. And that renders the existence of the world impossible entirely, because it depends on what is impossible to do and to come into existence. And if the cause, thought, motive, motivation, and mover are brought into existence without a cause, and [if] they are more fitting for non-existence than for existence without a cause... and if the agent is wise, not foolish, then it is possible for other events to occur from Him without a cause, and He would be wise, not foolish. And His departure from foolishness by bringing into existence a single event without a cause is not more fitting than His departure from it by bringing into existence all events without a cause. And this invalidates what they supposed, a clear invalidation.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a reductio argument against two possibilities for the cause of the world. First, if every cause requires another cause ad infinitum, the world would never come to be—an impossibility. Second, if the cause is without a cause and the agent is wise, then the agent could produce all events without cause, making every event arbitrary; this is also absurd. Thus the only valid position is a first cause acting with wisdom and purpose.

C) The first cause would be uncaused.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's argument shows that an infinite regress of causes leads to impossibility, and an uncaused cause without wisdom leads to absurdity; therefore, a first wise cause is necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If an infinite regress of causes is impossible, then your very existence demands an ultimate, uncaused Cause. Are you living today as if your life is just a random link in an endless chain, or are you consciously aligning your will with the Wise Creator who gave you existence? Where do you need to stop seeking secondary causes and turn directly to Him?
?
Baqillani argues that wisdom is shown when an agent does not act arbitrarily. God's wisdom means everything He does has a purpose, even if you don't see it. What event in your life right now seems pointless or unfair? Will you trust that His wisdom is behind it, or will you insist on a cause you can understand?
?
The second horn of the argument warns that if God could create one event without a cause, then all events could be without cause, leading to chaos. When you sin, do you treat it as an isolated event without consequence, or do you confess that it disrupts your entire relationship with the First Cause? What would it mean to root out every small rebellion as if it could unravel everything?

Refutation of the World's Maker as a Nature: The Dilemma of Non-Existent vs. Existent Nature
Baqillani presents a dilemma: the claimed nature must be either non-existent or existent, and he refutes the first possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's initial dilemma: if the world's maker is a nature, it must be either non-existent or existent; both options lead to rational problems.

Core Idea
A non-existent nature cannot cause anything; an existent nature must be either eternal or created.

Baqillani begins his refutation of those who claim the world's maker is a nature by posing a fundamental dilemma. The supposed nature cannot be both non-existent and existent at once. By examining each possibility, he shows that neither can coherently account for the world's existence. This lesson unpacks the first branch of this argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
Read the opening question: 'Why do you deny that the maker of the world could be a nature among natures, such that the world's occurrence is necessitated by its existence?' Recognise that the opponent posits a nature as the cause of the world.
The opponent is likely from a naturalist or materialist school.
2
Follow the first horn: non-existent nature
Baqillani argues that if the nature is non-existent, it cannot do anything, because that would allow any non-existent thing to cause events—absurd. Pay attention to the reasoning: the name 'non-existent' does not designate a substance or attribute, so it cannot be the source of acts.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
See the second horn introduced
If the nature is existent, then it must be either eternal or created. The passage cuts off here, but note that Baqillani sets up a new dilemma. The argument would likely show that an eternal nature necessitates eternal events, contradicting the temporality of the world.
This branch is incomplete in the source; we will continue in later lessons.

Chapter: Discussion on those who say [the world is by] the action of natures. If someone says: 'Why do you deny that the maker of the world could be a nature among natures, such that the world's occurrence is necessitated by its existence?' It is said to him: 'We deny that because this nature must be either an existing entity or a non-existent thing that is nothing. If it is non-existent, not a thing, then it cannot do anything, or that anything comes from it, or be attributed to it, because if that were permissible, then the existence of events from every non-existent and from every non-existent would be permissible. For what falls under this name is not a substance and is not distinguished by any rulings or attributes. So if it could cause acts or be necessary for them, that would be valid for every non-existent, and that is false by consensus. And if the nature to which the questioner attributes the occurrence of the world and its attachment is an existing entity, then that nature, which according to them necessitates the world's occurrence, must be either eternal or created. If it is eternal, then the events that occur from it must be...'

If non-existent, it cannot cause anything, because that would allow any non-existent thing to cause events, which is absurd. If existent, then it must be either eternal or created. The passage cuts off at that point; but the argument would continue to show that an eternal nature would necessitate eternal events, contradicting the temporality of the world.

The Dilemma of Nature as Creator
Baqillani refutes the nature-as-creator claim with a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's logical refutation of the claim that nature is the creator of the world.

Core Idea
Nature cannot create: it either is nothing or would make the world eternal.

Baqillani presents a powerful dilemma to those who claim that the world's creator is a nature. He forces them to choose: is this nature something real or not? If not, it cannot cause anything. If it is real, is it eternal or temporal? Each option leads to a contradiction. Let's examine this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim being refuted
Some argue that the maker of the world is a nature (like fire or water) that necessarily produces the world. Baqillani begins by stating this position.
The claim is that nature acts necessarily, not by choice.
2
Follow the first horn: nature is non-existent
If the nature is non-existent (not a real thing), it cannot be the source of any action. Baqillani argues that attributing causation to a non-entity would allow anything to cause anything, which is absurd.
This horn shows that a non-existent cause is impossible.
3
Follow the second horn: nature is existent and eternal
If the nature is existent and eternal, then since nature acts necessarily without impediment, the world would have to coexist eternally with the nature. But both parties agree the world is temporal, so this is impossible.
The analogy of fire burning is used: fire always burns when present.
4
Conclude the refutation
Since both possibilities lead to absurdity, the claim that nature is the creator is false. The true creator must be a voluntary agent.
This argument establishes that only a being with will and power can create temporal things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام على القائلين بفعل الطباع

فإن قال قائل : لم أنكرتم أن يكون صانع العالم طبيعة من الطبائع وجب حدوث العالم عن وجودها؟ قيل له: أنكرنا ذلك لأن هذه الطبيعة لا تخلو أن تكون معنى موجودا أو معدوما ليس بشيء. فإن كانت معدومة ليست بشيء لم يجز أن تفعل شيئا أو أن يكون عنها شيء أو ينسب إليها شيء لأنه لو جاز ذلك جاز وجود الحوادث من كل معدوم وعن كل معدوم؛ لأن ما يقع عليه هذا الاسم فليس بذات ولا يختص ببعض الأحكام والصفات فلو كان منه ما يحدث الأفعال أو تجب عنه لصح ذلك من كل معدوم وذلك باطل باتفاق.

وإن كانت الطبيعة التي نسب إليها السائل حدوث العلم وعلقه بها معنى موجودا، لم تخل تلك الطبيعة الموجبة عندهم لحدوث العالم من أن تكون قديمة أو محدثة فإن كانت قديمة وجب أن تكون الحوادث الكائنة عنها قديمة لأن الطبيعة لم تزل موجودة ولا مانع من وجود الحوادث الموجبة عنها فيجب وجودها مع الطبيعة في القدم كما يجب اعتماد الحجر مع وجود طبعه، وإحراق النار وانحلال الطبع والإسكار مع وجود طبع النار والسقمونيا والشراب إذا لم يمنع من ذلك مانع؛ فكذلك يجب وجود العالم في القدم، وإن كان محدثا مع وجود الطبع الكائن عنه عندهم. إذا لم يمنع من ذلك مانع، وفي إطباقنا وإياهم على استحالة قدم الحوادث دليل على أنها لا يجوز أن تكون حادثة عن طبيعة قديمة.
Chapter of discussion on those who say [the agent is] the action of natures.

So if a questioner says: Why did you deny that the maker of the world could be a nature among the natures, and the occurrence of the world from its existence is necessary? It is said to him: We denied that because this nature does not fail to be either an existent meaning or a non-existent which is nothing. If it is non-existent, not a thing, it is not permissible that it do something, or that something come from it, or that something be attributed to it, because if that were permissible, the existence of temporal events from every non-existent and from every non-existent would be permissible; because what falls under this name is not a self and is not distinct by some rulings and attributes. So if it were possible that something produce actions or that actions be necessitated from it, that would be valid from every non-existent, and that is false by agreement.

And if the nature to which the questioner attributed the occurrence of the world and its attachment is an existent meaning, that nature which (according to them) necessitates the occurrence of the world does not escape from being either eternal or temporal. If it is eternal, then the temporal events that occur from it must be eternal, because the nature has never ceased to exist and there is no impediment preventing the existence of the temporal events necessitated by it, so their existence with the nature in eternity is necessary, just as the downward motion of a stone is necessary with the existence of its nature, and the burning of fire, the dissolution of [a substance's] nature, and intoxication with the existence of the nature of fire, scammony, and wine when there is no impediment to that. Likewise, the existence of the world would be necessary in eternity, even if it were temporal with the existence of the nature that produced it according to them when there is no impediment to that. And our unanimous agreement with them on the impossibility of the eternity of temporal events is evidence that it is not permissible for them to occur from an eternal nature.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who claim that the creator of the world is a nature. Baqillani structures the argument as a dilemma: the proposed nature either is non-existent or existent. If non-existent, it cannot cause anything, because attributing causation to a non-entity would imply that anything could arise from nothing—absurd. If existent, then it is either eternal or temporal. If eternal, then since a nature (like fire causing burning) necessitates its effect whenever it exists without impediment, the world would have to coexist eternally with the nature. But both parties agree that the world is temporal (hadith) and that eternal temporal events are impossible. Therefore, the nature cannot be eternal. If the nature is temporal, it would itself need a cause, leading to infinite regress. Thus, the creator cannot be a nature. The key move is the analogy: just as fire necessarily burns when present, an eternal nature would necessitate the eternal existence of the world.

Key Terms
طبيعة
قديم
محدث
علة
إيجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Baqillani argued that nature cannot be eternal because it would cause temporal events eternally; but the argument is that an eternal nature would necessitate the world's eternal existence, which is impossible.
Correction: The dilemma shows that an eternal nature would require the world to be eternal, contradicting its temporal nature. The impossibility is of a temporal effect from an eternal cause.

A) It lacks will
B) It is not a self and attributing action to it would allow anything from nothing
C) It is temporal
D) It is not powerful

Key Takeaway
Baqillani shows that the concept of nature as creator leads to logical absurdities, confirming that the creator must be a voluntary, eternal agent.

Impossible Origin: World from Eternal Nature
Argument that an eternal nature would necessitate an eternal world, contradicting agreed impossibility of eternal temporal events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reductio ad absurdum argument against the claim that the world is caused by an eternal nature, and how the analogies of natural phenomena demonstrate the necessitarian relationship between cause and effect.

Core Idea
An eternal cause must produce an eternal effect, so the world cannot be temporal if caused by an eternal nature.

This passage presents a powerful argument from Imam al-Baqillani against the philosophers who claim the world is produced by an eternal nature. He argues that if such a nature exists, it must necessitate its effect eternally—like fire burns immediately. But all parties agree that temporal events cannot be eternal. This contradiction shows the impossibility of an eternal nature causing the temporal world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the initial question
The author begins by implying a question: Is the nature that produces the world eternal? Read the passage once to get the gist.
Pay attention to the logical flow: if eternal, then what follows?
2
Understand the causal principle
An eternal nature with no impediment must necessitate its effect eternally. The author states this directly: 'nature has always existed and there is no impediment... so their existence with nature in eternity is necessary'.
This is the key premise of the argument.
3
Analyze the analogies
The author gives examples: a stone's weight necessarily causes it to fall (unless prevented), fire necessarily burns, scammony purges, wine intoxicates. These show how natures necessitate effects.
Each analogy reinforces the idea that an eternal nature would necessitate an eternal effect.
4
Apply the reasoning to the world
If the world is caused by an eternal nature, and there is no impediment, the world must also be eternal. But the philosophers themselves agree that temporal events (like the world) cannot be eternal.
This creates a contradiction.
5
Conclude the impossibility
Since the agreed premise (temporal events cannot be eternal) contradicts the consequence of an eternal nature, the world cannot have originated from an eternal nature. This refutes the philosophers' claim.
The argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قديمة لأن الطبيعة لم تَزَل موجودة ولا مانع من وجود الحوادث الموجبة عنها فيجب وجودها مع الطبيعة في القدم كما يجب اعتماد الحجر مع وجود طَبْعِه، وإحراق النار وانحلال الطبع والإسكار مع وجود طبع النار والسقمونيا والشراب إذا لم يمنع من ذلك مانع؛ فكذلك يجب وجود العالم في القِدَم، وإن كان مُحْدَثاً مع وجود الطبع الكائن عنه عندهم. إذا لم يمنع من ذلك مانع، وفي إطباقنا وإياهم على استحالة قدم الحوادث دليل على أنها لا يجوز أن تكون حادثة عن طبيعة قديمة.
Eternal? Because nature has always existed and there is no impediment to the existence of the events necessitated by it, so their existence with nature in eternity is necessary just as the downward movement of a stone with the existence of its weight, and the burning of fire, and the dissolution of [؟] and intoxication with the existence of the nature of fire and scammony and wine, if nothing prevents it, are necessary. Likewise, the existence of the world in eternity would be necessary, and it would be temporal with the existence of the nature that causes it according to them, if nothing prevents it. And our agreement and their agreement on the impossibility of the eternity of temporal events is evidence that they cannot be originated from an eternal nature.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents an argument against the view that the world is caused by an eternal nature (tabi'a). The author begins by stating what would follow if the world were produced by an eternal nature: since nature necessitates its effect immediately and eternally (like the weight of a stone causing it to fall, fire causing burning, scammony causing purging, etc., unless prevented), the world would have to exist eternally alongside the nature. But the philosophers themselves agree that temporal events (hawadith) cannot be eternal. Therefore, the world cannot be originated from an eternal nature. This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Key Terms
طبيعة (tabi'a, nature)
قديم (qadim, eternal)
حادث (hadith, temporal/originated)
حوادث (hawadith, temporal events)
مانع (mani', impediment)
موجبة (mujiba, necessitating)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if an eternal cause exists, its effect must be eternal, but that would be fine because the world could still be temporal if the cause is eternal and the effect is temporal.
Correction: The author argues that an eternal nature, by its nature, necessitates its effect eternally. If the effect is temporal, the nature cannot be eternal. The impossibility of eternal temporal events is used to refute the claim that the world is from an eternal nature.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what is the consequence of the world being caused by an eternal nature?
A) The world would be temporal.
B) The world would be eternal.
C) The world would be impossible.
D) The world would be contingent.

Key Takeaway
The argument demonstrates that an eternal nature would necessarily produce an eternal world, but since temporal events cannot be eternal, the world cannot have been caused by an eternal nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see the patterns of nature around you—like fire burning or water flowing—do you ever pause to consider that these are not necessary from an eternal nature but are sustained moment by moment by a willing Creator? The argument shows the world cannot be from blind nature. Does this truth shape your awareness of God's constant involvement in your life?
?
This passage proves that temporal events cannot be eternal. Your own life is a series of temporal events: your health, your wealth, your relationships. If you truly believed that none of these can be from an eternal nature, how would your attachment to them change? Would you still cling to them as if they are permanent?
?
The analogy of the stone falling due to its weight illustrates natural necessity. But your own actions are not like that—you have free will. The argument implies that the ultimate cause of the world is not a blind force but a Will. If God's will is the source of all temporal events, then why do you sometimes live as if your circumstances are inevitable and beyond God's control? What would it mean for you to turn to Him in every situation?

Impossibility of Infinite Regress of Temporal Natures
Argument that a temporal nature leads to an impossible infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a temporal cause for the world would lead to an impossible infinite regress of natures.

Core Idea
A temporal nature cannot produce the world without an infinite regress.

Imam Baqillani presents a powerful argument against the idea that the nature producing the world could be temporal. If the nature itself is temporal, it would require another prior nature, leading to an infinite chain that cannot actually exist. This would make the world's existence impossible. Let's explore his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
The argument starts by assuming the nature that produces the world is itself temporal (originated). Grasp this starting point.
This is the assumption to be refuted.
2
Follow the first branch
Consider the case where the temporal nature arises from another nature. What does that imply?
This leads to a chain.
3
Recognize the infinite regress
If the chain continues indefinitely without an end, you have an infinite regress of natures.
Baqillani says 'likewise indefinitely without end'.
4
Understand the impossibility
An infinite series cannot actually come into existence; it is impossible. This is compared to the impossibility of motion and rest coexisting.
This is the key logical move.
5
Conclude the absurdity
If the world's existence depends on an impossible infinite chain, then the world would be impossible. Since the world exists, the original assumption must be false.
Thus the nature producing the world is not temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان الطبعُ المُحْدِثُ للعالم مُحْدَثاً فلا يخلو أن يكون حادثاً عن طبع أو لا عن طبع، فإن كان حادثاً عن طبع أوجبته وجب أيضاً أن تكون تلك الطبيعة كائنة حادثة عن طبيعة أخرى أوجبتها، وكذلك القول في طبع الطبع أبداً إلى غير غاية؛ وهذا يُحيل وجود العالم لأنه متعلق بوجود ما لا غاية له وقد ثبت استحالة خروج ما لا غاية له إلى الوجود كما ثبتت استحالة اجتماع الحركة عن المكان والسكون في المحل الواحد معاً، فكما تجب استحالة وجود العالم وحدوثه لو عُلّق باجتماع الحركة عن المكان والسكون.
And if the nature that produces the world is itself temporal, then it does not escape being either originated from a nature or not from a nature. If it is originated from a nature that necessitated it, then that nature must also be a temporal originated thing from another nature that necessitated it, and likewise regarding the nature of that nature indefinitely without end. This renders the existence of the world impossible because it is dependent on the existence of that which has no end, and it has been established that it is impossible for that which has no end to come into existence, just as it has been established that it is impossible for motion from place and rest to coexist in one locus. So just as the existence and temporal origination of the world would be impossible if linked to the simultaneous occurrence of motion from place and rest.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents an argument against the view that the nature producing the world is itself temporal. If the nature is temporal, it must either come from another nature or not. If it comes from another nature, that nature must also be temporal, leading to an infinite regress of natures. That is impossible because an infinite series cannot actually exist (like motion and rest cannot coexist). Therefore, the existence of the world would be impossible, which is absurd. Hence the initial premise (that the nature is temporal) is false. Alternatively, if the nature is not from a nature, then it must be from something else, which would be the Creator.

Key Terms
مُحْدِث (muhdith, producer)
حادث (hadith, temporal)
طبيعة (tabi'a)
غاية (ghaya, end)
استحالة (istihala, impossibility)
تسلسل (tasalsul, infinite regress)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think a temporal nature could be self-sufficient without requiring a prior cause.
Correction: But if it is temporal, it must have a cause. If that cause is another nature, the chain continues infinitely, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion if the nature producing the world is itself temporal?
A) The world is eternal.
B) There is an infinite regress of natures, which is impossible.
C) The world is created by God.
D) Nature is eternal.

Key Takeaway
A temporal cause would lead to an impossible infinite regress, proving the world's cause must be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that an infinite regress of causes is impossible and that an eternal cause must exist, does your daily life reflect a deep trust in that eternal cause, or do you still act as if things are random and purposeless? What would change if you lived with constant awareness of the eternal cause behind every moment?
?
The passage shows that temporal things cannot be the ultimate source of existence. Consider the temporary things you rely on for security—your health, wealth, or relationships. How does knowing that only an eternal cause can truly sustain anything affect your attachment to these temporary things? What is one attachment you can begin to loosen today?
?
In what area of your life do you tend to assume that things happen by mere chance or without a deeper purpose? How can the logical certainty of an eternal cause reshape that assumption, and what is one concrete step you can take this week to align your thinking with the truth that the world's cause is eternal and purposeful?

Divine Power vs Natural Necessitation
Baqillani distinguishes God's power from natural necessitation in causing effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental distinction between God's power (qudra) and natural necessitation, and why Baqillani argues that divine power is not a cause or necessitator.

Core Idea
God’s power is free, not compelled; nature necessitates, God chooses.

Imam Baqillani presents a crisp contrast between the Islamic view of divine causation and the naturalistic view. He argues that God's power does not force the possible into existence but rather acts freely, while nature, as claimed by some, compels its effects. This passage lays the groundwork for understanding why kalam rejects any necessary causal links between created things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall argument. Note how Baqillani uses terms like قدرة, طبع, موجب.
2
Identify the two contrasting descriptions
Look for how Baqillani describes God's power (ليس بعلة ولا موجبة) and how he describes nature (موجب لما يحدث عنه). Understand the key difference.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Baqillani ends with بان الفرق بين قولنا وقولكم. This is the point: the distinction is clear and foundational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لمقدوره فاعل بها وإن كانت على صفة ما يصح أن يفعل بها، وكان هو تعالى على صفة من يصح أن يفعل بها، وكان المعدوم المقدور مما يصح أن يخرج إلى الوجود ولا مانع يمنع من خروجه لأن قدرته ليست بعلة ولا موجبة له؛ وأنتم تزعمون أن الطبع الكائن عنه العالم وكل طبع كان منه أمر من الأمور موجب لما يحدث عنه ومقتض له، إذا لم يمنع من ذلك مانع، فبان الفرق بين قولنا وقولكم.
His power is an agent of it (the possible), even if it is upon a description that makes it valid to act by it, and He, exalted, is upon a description of one who can act by it, and the non-existent possible is something that can come into existence and no preventer prevents its coming out because His power is not a cause nor a necessitator of it; while you claim that the nature from which the world comes to be, and every nature from which a thing arises, is a necessitator of what occurs from it and a requirer of it, if no preventer prevents that. So the distinction between our statement and yours becomes clear.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational distinction in kalam between divine power and natural causation. Baqillani argues that God's power (qudra) is not a cause ('illa) nor a necessitator (mujiba) for the existence of the possible (maqdūr). Rather, it is an agent that acts without necessitation, and the possible object remains possible until God chooses to create it. In contrast, the naturalists (those who posit a 'nature' or tab') claim that nature necessarily produces its effect—if nothing prevents it. Baqillani concludes that this difference clearly separates the Islamic position from that of the philosophers or materialists. The argument is part of a larger refutation of naturalistic theories of causation.

Key Terms
قدرة
طبع
موجب
علة
مقدور
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's power necessitates creation like nature.
Correction: Baqillani stresses that God's power is free and not necessitating, while nature is a necessitator.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the key difference between God's power and nature?
A) God's power is a cause, nature is not.
B) God's power is not a cause or necessitator, while nature is a necessitator.
C) Both are necessitators but God's is eternal.
D) Nature is free, God's power is constrained.

Key Takeaway
God’s power does not necessitate; it is free agency, while nature necessitates its effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God owes you something because of your obedience or prayers? How does Baqillani's insistence that God's power is not a cause or necessitator challenge that assumption? Will you now ask Him with true humility, not expectation of compulsion?
?
If God's power is not necessitous, then your existence is a free gift, not an automatic result. Does your daily life reflect the weight of that gift, or do you live as if you were entitled to be created? What would change if you truly believed He could have left you non-existent?
?
When you see the order of nature, do you secretly believe it runs on its own, like a machine? Baqillani says nature necessitates, but God's power does not. Does your heart rely more on natural routines than on the free, personal act of God each moment? How can you reorient yourself to see His hand in everything?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
8 min
Refutation of a Created Nature Causing the World
Baqillani refutes the idea of a created nature causing the world by infinite regress and alternative arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the claim that a created nature (tab') is the cause of the world's existence, using the impossibility of infinite regress and alternative logical possibilities.

Core Idea
A created nature cannot cause the world without leading to absurdity or infinite regress.

Baqillani tackles a key objection from naturalists who claim that a 'nature' (tab') is the real cause behind the world's events. He systematically dismantles this idea by showing that if the nature is created, it either leads to an infinite chain of causes or becomes unnecessary altogether. This passage is a masterclass in reductio ad absurdum and logical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Get a feel for the flow of Baqillani's arguments.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...' used repeatedly.
2
Identify the first scenario: nature as a living agent
Baqillani first addresses the objection about God's will. He then considers if the proponents claim their nature is a living, powerful, choosing agent. Note his response: they agree with the truth but differ in name.
This is a strategic concession that undermines their position.
3
Follow the infinite regress argument
If the nature is created, and it is created from another nature, that leads to an infinite regress. Baqillani argues this is impossible because it would make the world's existence dependent on an endless chain.
He compares this to the impossibility of motion and rest coexisting.
4
Consider the alternative: nature created without a prior nature
If the nature is created not from a nature, then there is no need to posit a nature for the world's origination. Baqillani uses this to show the proponents' position is self-defeating.
5
Reflect on the originator of nature
Baqillani further examines the originator of the nature: if created by a nature, regress; if without a nature, then nature is unnecessary; if the originator is eternal with an eternal nature, the world would be eternal.
This closes all logical possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك الجواب إن ألزموا هذا الإلزام في إرادة الله تعالى للأفعال وإن كانت قديمة عندنا لأنها على قولنا إرادة لكون الفعل على التراخي ولأنها ليست علة لوجود المراد فإن قالوا: إن هذا الطبع القديم هو شيء حي عالم قادر ليس بموجب للفعل ولا علة له، بل يفعل بالقدرة والاختيار أقروا بالحق وصانع العالم الذي نثبته، وإن خالفونا في تسميته طبعاً، وكان هذا عندنا محظوراً بالشرع. وإن كان الطبع المحدث للعالم محدثاً فلا يخلو أن يكون حادثاً عن طبع أو لا عن طبع، فإن كان حادثاً عن طبع أوجبه وجب أيضاً أن تكون تلك الطبيعة كائنة حادثة عن طبيعة أخرى أوجبتها، وكذلك القول في طبع الطبع أبداً إلى غير غاية؛ وهذا يحيل وجود العالم لأنه متعلق بوجود ما لا غاية له وقد ثبت استحالة خروج ما لا غاية له إلى الوجود كما ثبتت استحالة اجتماع الحركة عن المكان والسكون في المحل الواحد معاً فكما تجب استحالة وجود العالم وحدوثه لو عُلّق باجتماع الحركة عن المكان والسكون فيه أيضاً معاً، فلذلك أيضاً تجب استحالة وجوده وحدوثه إذا عُلّق بوجود طبائع هي حوادث لا غاية لها، وفي صحة وجود العالم وحدوثه دليل على فساد هذا القول. وإن كانت الطبيعة الموجبة لحدوث العالم حادثة لا عن طبيعة أوجبتها جاز أيضاً حدوث العالم لا عن طبيعة أوجبته، وحدوث الإسكار والإحراق والتبريد والتسخين وسائر الحوادث لا عن طبيعة؛ كما أنه لو جاز حدوث محدث واحد لا من محدث لجاز حدوث سائر الحوادث لا من محدث؛ وهذا يبطل قولهم بإثبات طبيعة حدث العالم عنها. وعلى أن هذه الطبيعة إن كانت محدثةً فلا تخلو أن يكون أحدثها محدث أو لا فإن كانت حادثة عن محدث فلا تخلو أن يكون أحدثها محدثها بطبع أو بغير طبع فإن كان أحدثها بطبع وكان طبعه أيضاً محدثاً وجب أن يكون لطبعه محدث ولمحدثه طبع محدث له محدث أبداً إلى غير غاية وذلك محال، وإن كان محدث الطبيعة أحدثها بغير طبع جاز حدوث العالم أيضاً من محدث ليس بذي طبع وبطل إثبات الطبع. وإن كان محدث الطبيعة التي حدث العالم عنها قديماً وكان طبعه قديماً وجب قدم الطبيعة وقدم العالم الكائن عنها على ما بيناه من قبل هذا وهذا ظاهر في أنه لا يجوز أن يكون العالم حادثاً عن طبع من الطبائع.
And similarly the answer if they impose this objection regarding the will of God, exalted, for actions, even if it is eternal according to us, because according to our statement it is a will for the action to occur after delay (tarākhī), and because it is not a cause for the existence of the object of will. For if they say: 'This eternal nature is a living, knowing, powerful thing, not a necessitator of the action nor a cause of it, but rather it acts by power and choice,' they affirm the truth and the Maker of the world that we affirm, even if they disagree with us in calling it 'nature,' and this is prohibited by the Law according to us. And if the nature that brings about the world is created, then it is either originated from a nature or not from a nature. If it is originated from a nature that necessitated it, then that nature must also be originated from another nature that necessitated it, and so on ad infinitum. This renders the existence of the world impossible because it is dependent on the existence of that which has no end, and it has been established that it is impossible for that which has no end to come into existence, just as it is impossible for motion from a place and rest to coexist in a single locus together. Therefore, just as the existence and origination of the world would be impossible if they were dependent on the coexistence of motion from a place and rest in it together, so also the existence and origination of the world must be impossible if they are dependent on the existence of natures that are originated without end. And the fact that the world's existence and origination are sound is evidence for the falsity of this statement. And if the nature that necessitates the origination of the world is originated not from a nature that necessitated it, then it would also be permissible for the world to occur without a nature that necessitated it, and for the occurrence of intoxication, burning, cooling, heating, and all events to occur without a nature. Just as if it were permissible for one originated thing to occur without an originator, it would be permissible for all events to occur without an originator. This invalidates their claim of affirming a nature from which the world occurred. Moreover, if this nature is created, then either an originator originated it or not. If it is originated from an originator, then either that originator originated it by a nature or not by a nature. If he originated it by a nature and his nature is also created, then he must have an originator for his nature, and that originator has a created nature, and so on ad infinitum, which is impossible. And if the originator of the nature originated it without a nature, then it is permissible for the world to occur from an originator without a nature, and the affirmation of nature is invalidated. And if the originator of the nature from which the world occurred is eternal and his nature is eternal, then the nature would be eternal and the world coming from it would be eternal, as we explained before this. And this is evident: it is not permissible for the world to be originated from any nature at all.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation of the notion that the world is caused by a 'nature' (tab'). Baqillani presents several arguments. First, he addresses the objection concerning God's will: even if God's will is eternal, it is not a cause but a will for delayed action. If the proponents of nature claim that their nature is a living, powerful, choosing agent, then they agree with the Islamic position in essence, differing only in name – but this is prohibited by Law. Then he tackles the scenario where nature is created. If it is created by another nature, that leads to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible because it would require an infinite chain of causes, and the world's existence is contingent on such a chain, which is absurd. He compares this to the impossibility of motion and rest coexisting in one locus. Since the world does exist, the premise of an infinite regress of natures must be false. If, on the other hand, the nature is created without a preceding nature, then there is no need to posit a nature for the world's origination – it could originate directly from an originator without nature. This also undermines their theory. Additionally, if the creator of the nature created it by a nature, that leads to regress; if without nature, then again nature is unnecessary. Finally, if the creator of the nature is eternal and his nature is eternal, then the nature and its effect (the world) would be eternal, contradicting the observed temporality (hudūth) of the world. Therefore, the world cannot be originated from any nature. This is a classic kalam argument against naturalistic causation, using the principle of impossibility of infinite regress and the observed fact of the world's origination.

Key Terms
طبع
محدث
حادث
علة
موجب
تسلسل
إرادة
تراخي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Baqillani denies any secondary causality; but here he specifically refutes the idea of a necessitating nature, not all causal relationships.
Correction: Baqillani's target is the concept of a nature (tab') that necessarily produces effects, not the idea of causal relationships per se.

Checkpoint
What is one of the reasons Baqillani gives to reject the claim that a created nature caused the world?
A) Because nature is eternal and cannot cause a temporal world.
B) Because if nature is created by another nature, it leads to an infinite regress, which is impossible.
C) Because nature is an accident and cannot be a substance.
D) Because God's will is the direct cause of everything.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani shows that positing a created nature as the cause of the world leads either to infinite regress or renders the nature superfluous, thus proving that the world cannot originate from any nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see daily events like the sun rising or fire burning, do your thoughts automatically attribute them to 'nature' rather than to Allah's direct command? What does this reveal about the state of your tawheed? Pause right now and deliberately attribute one natural event you experience today to the immediate act of Allah, not to its physical cause. Will you do this consciously every time you encounter a causal event today?
?
Baqillani argues that positing a created nature leads to infinite regress or makes nature superfluous. In your own life, do you unconsciously treat created means (e.g., your job, skills, or health) as independent causes of success? How does this argument expose the hidden shirk of attributing power to these 'natures'? Make a commitment tonight to thank Allah directly for one outcome you normally credit to a secondary cause.
?
The infinite regress argument cuts off any excuse for an endless chain of causes. Are there areas of your life where you justify your actions by an never-ending chain of reasons, never referencing Allah as the ultimate cause? For example, 'I did this because of that, and that because of this...' Today, stop at one chain and explicitly recognize Allah as the First Cause. How will you train yourself to rest on Allah's will as the foundation?

Refutation of the Four Natures Theory: First Argument
Baqillani begins refuting the four-natures theory with the first of several arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's first argument against the theory that the world is composed of the four natures: heat, cold, moisture, and dryness.

Core Idea
The four 'natures' are opposing accidents, not eternal substances.

Baqillani refutes the ancient theory that the world is built from four elementary qualities. He begins by showing that these qualities—heat, cold, moisture, and dryness—are not fundamental principles but are created accidents that cannot coexist in the same subject, proving they are temporal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp Baqillani's opening claim.
The passage is incomplete; it cuts off mid-argument.
2
Identify the four natures
Note the four qualities: heat, cold, moisture, and dryness. Baqillani declares their composition theory false.
3
Understand the key claim
Baqillani asserts these natures are accidents (a'rād), created (muhdatha), and mutually opposed (mutadādda). He gives the example that heat and cold cannot coexist in one locus.
This shows they cannot be eternal principles.
4
Grasp the logical implication
If heat and cold cannot coexist, then when one appears, the other must have ceased. This implies they are temporal events, not eternal.
The argument would continue with further reasons, but the text ends here.
5
Reflect on the incomplete argument
Notice that only the first 'face' (وجه) of the refutation is given. The full argument requires subsequent pages.
This lesson covers only the beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قول من قال : إن العالم بأسره مركب من الطبائع الأربع : الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة فإنه باطل من وجوه أحدها أن هذه الطبائع أعراض مُحْدَثة متضادّة على الأجسام لأنه محال اجتماع الحرارة مع البرودة في محلّ واحد فيجب حدوث الحرارة بعد بطلان البرودة وكذلك الرطوبة بعد
As for the statement of those who say that the entire world is composed of the four natures: heat, cold, moisture, and dryness, it is false from many aspects. One of them is that these natures are accidents, created, and mutually opposed (mutadādda) in bodies, because it is impossible for heat and cold to coexist in a single locus. Therefore, heat must occur after the cessation of cold, and similarly moisture after [the cessation of dryness]...
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage begins a new refutation, this time targeting the theory that the world is composed of the four primary qualities (heat, cold, moisture, dryness) as fundamental natures. Baqillani declares this view false and offers multiple proofs, but the text is cut off mid-first proof. The first point (first 'face') is that these supposedly fundamental natures are actually accidents (a'rād) – they inhere in bodies. Moreover, they are created (muhdatha) and mutually contradictory (mutadādda). For example, heat and cold cannot coexist in the same subject; if one occurs, the other must cease. This implies they are temporal events, not eternal principles. The argument would continue with further reasons, but the page ends here. This is a standard kalam argument against the materialist/elemental theories of the world.

Key Terms
طبائع أربع
أعراض
مُحْدَثة
متضادّة
حرارة
برودة
رطوبة
يبوسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the four natures are substances or eternal principles.
Correction: Baqillani stresses they are accidents, not substances, and they are created and mutually opposed, so they cannot be the eternal building blocks of the world.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the first problem with the four-natures theory?
A) They are eternal and cannot cause temporal things.
B) They are accidents, not substances, and they are mutually opposed.
C) They are not really four but many.
D) They are unknowable.

Key Takeaway
The four natures are not fundamental principles but are accidents that are created and mutually opposed, proving they cannot be the world's eternal building blocks.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Examine one area of your life where you might be acting as if 'heat' and 'cold' are independent eternal principles rather than Allah's created accidents. What would change if you saw them as Allah's direct creation every moment?
?
This lesson shows that even learned philosophers can be mistaken about the fundamental nature of reality. Is there a belief you hold about the world that you have never questioned because it seems 'scientific' or 'natural'? Identify one such belief and test it against the truth that everything is created by Allah, not self-existent.
?
The argument uses the mutual exclusivity of opposites to show they cannot be eternal. In your spiritual life, can two opposing states coexist simultaneously? For example, can true humility and pride coexist? Reflect on a recent situation where you felt a tension between two spiritual states. Which one must cease for the other to flourish? How does this reflect the temporal nature of your own soul?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
Refutation of the Four Natures Theory
Baqillani refutes the claim that the world is composed of the four primary qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that the four primary qualities cannot be eternal natures that compose the world.

Core Idea
The four qualities are temporal accidents, not eternal natures.

This passage is a classic refutation of an ancient theory that the world is made of four eternal qualities (heat, cold, moisture, dryness). Baqillani shows that these qualities are contradictory and temporal, so they cannot be eternal principles. As you read, notice how he uses logical reasoning and analogy to other accidents to make his case.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim being refuted
Read the first sentence to see the theory Baqillani is attacking: that the entire world is composed of the four natures. Identify the four natures listed.
This theory was common among Greek philosophers.
2
Examine the first argument
Baqillani points out that these natures are accidents (aʿrāḍ) that are contradictory. Heat and cold cannot coexist, so they must occur one after the other. This shows they are temporal, not eternal.
An accident cannot exist without a body; here they are said to inhere in bodies.
3
Compare to other contradictory accidents
Baqillani compares these qualities to motion/rest and blackness/whiteness. Just as these are temporal and cannot be eternal, the four natures are also temporal.
This strengthens the argument by showing consistency across all accidents.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that these natures cannot be eternal or parts of an eternal universal. Therefore, they are not real natures in the sense of eternal principles, and the world can be temporal without them.
This refutes the claim that the world must have an eternal nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قول من قال : إن العالم بأسره مركب من الطبائع الأربع : الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة فإنه باطل من وجوه. أحدها أن هذه الطبائع أعراض مُحْدَثة متضادّة على الأجسام لأنه محال اجتماع الحرارة مع البرودة في محلّ واحد فيجب حدوث الحرارة بعد بطلان البرودة وكذلك الرطوبة بعد اليبوسة فهذه الطبائع جارية مجرى الحركة والسكون والسواد والبياض وسائر الأعراض المتضادة فيجب حدوثها واستحالة كونها قديمةً أو بعضاً لكلّية حرارة وبرودة ورطوبة ويبوسة قديمة أزلية لأن المُحدث لا يجوز أن يكون بعضاً للقديم كما لا يجوز أن تكون جزئيات الحركة والسكون عن كليات قديمة فوجب القضاء على حدوث هذه الطبائع وأنها غير طبائع فكذلك جائز حدوث سائر العالم عن غير طبيعة.
As for the statement of one who says: The entire world is composed of the four natures: heat, cold, moisture, and dryness—it is false in several ways. One of them is that these natures are originated accidents, contradictory upon bodies, because it is impossible for heat and cold to coexist in one place, so heat must occur after the cessation of cold, and similarly moisture after dryness. So these natures run along the lines of motion and rest, blackness and whiteness, and all other contradictory accidents. Thus, their temporality is necessary and their being eternal or being a part of a universal of heat, cold, moisture, and dryness that is eternal and beginningless is impossible, because the originated cannot be a part of the eternal, just as it is not permissible for particulars of motion and rest to be from eternal universals. So it is necessary to judge that these natures are temporal and that they are not natures, and likewise it is permissible for the rest of the world to occur without a nature.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the ancient theory that the world is composed of the four primary qualities (heat, cold, moisture, dryness). Baqillani argues that these qualities are accidents (aʿrāḍ) that are originated and contradictory. For example, heat and cold cannot coexist in one subject, so they succeed each other temporally. He compares them to other accidental pairs like motion/rest and blackness/whiteness, all of which are temporally successive. Therefore, these natures cannot be eternal, nor can they be parts of an eternal universal (kulliyya qadīma), because an originated thing cannot be part of the eternal. Hence, these are not real natures in the sense of eternal principles, and their temporality allows the world to be temporal without any nature.

متضادة
كلية قديمة
جزئيات

Misconception: The four natures (heat, cold, etc.) are eternal principles that compose the world.
Correction: Baqillani shows they are temporal accidents that cannot coexist, so they cannot be eternal. They are not natures in the sense of eternal principles.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani say the four natures cannot be eternal?
A) Because they are created by God.
B) Because they are contradictory and succeed each other in time.
C) Because they are not found in the world.
D) Because the world is not made of them.

Key Takeaway
The four primary qualities are temporal, contradictory accidents, not eternal natures; therefore, the world does not need an eternal nature to exist.

?
Baqillani argues that an originated thing cannot be part of the eternal. Look at your own life: what attachments or habits do you treat as permanent that are actually temporary? How might accepting their temporality free you to focus on what truly lasts?
?
The four natures theory tries to explain the world without the Creator. Are there areas in your life where you rely on 'natural' explanations or excuses instead of turning to Allah? How can you reorient your perspective to see His hand in every change?

Accidents Cannot Be Agents
Baqillani argues that accidents cannot be agents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani denies that accidents can be the agents of the world, based on the necessary attributes of a true agent.

Core Idea
A true agent must be alive, knowing, powerful, and intending.

In this passage, Baqillani builds on his earlier demonstration that the four qualities are accidents. He then argues that accidents cannot perform actions because a genuine agent must possess specific attributes. This establishes that the world cannot be produced by mere accidental natures.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the conditional structure.
2
Identify the attributes of an agent
Find the list of attributes Baqillani says a true agent must have: living, knowing, powerful, intending.
These are the four necessary qualities.
3
Apply to the naturalists' claim
Realize that accidents lack these attributes, so they cannot be the agents of the world.
4
Draw the conclusion
Baqillani concludes that the natures (accidents) cannot be agents of the world.
This refutes the naturalists' position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى أنا قد بينا أن هذه الأجناس أعراض وبينا أن الأعراض لا يجوز أن تفعل شيئاً لأن الفاعل لا يكون إلا حيّاً عالماً قادراً قاصداً إذا كان فعله مُحْكَماً فلم يَجُزْ أن تكون هذه الطبائع فاعلة للعالم. ومما يدل على استحالة قدم هذه الطبائع أنها لو كانت قديمة، وكان العالم حادثاً عنها، لوجب قدمه مع قدمها على ما بيناه من قبل إذ لا مانع يمنع من كونه معها. فإن قالوا : كذلك نقول قيل لهم : فإذا كان الطبع قديماً أزلياً، وكان الكائن عنه قديماً أزلياً، فَلِمَ كان أحدهما بأن يكون موجباً للآخر وسبباً له أولى من أن يكون المُسَبَّبُ سبباً وعلةً؟ فلا يجدون في ذلك مُتَعَلَّقاً.
And moreover, we have already shown that these genera are accidents, and we have shown that accidents cannot do anything, because the agent cannot be except living, knowing, powerful, intending when his action is precise; so it is not permissible for these natures to be agents of the world. And among what indicates the impossibility of the eternity of these natures is that if they were eternal, and the world were temporal from them, then its eternity would be necessary with their eternity, as we have explained before, since there is no impediment preventing it from being with them. If they say: 'We say the same,' it is said to them: 'If the nature is eternal and beginningless, and the thing coming from it is eternal and beginningless, then why is one of them more deserving to be the necessitator and cause of the other than the caused being a cause and a ground?' They will find no support in that.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage combines two distinct arguments. First, Baqillani reiterates that the four qualities are accidents, and accidents cannot perform actions because a true agent (fāʿil) must be living, knowing, powerful, and intending (especially for precise, well-ordered acts). Since these qualities lack these attributes, they cannot be the agents that produced the world. Second, he argues that if these natures were eternal, then the world arising from them would also be eternal, because there is no impediment to its existence with them. If the naturalists counter that they accept the world is eternal, Baqillani retorts: if both nature and its effect are eternal, why is the nature considered the cause and the world the effect? The relationship could be reversed, or they could be independent. Thus, the naturalists' position is arbitrary and unsupported.

Key Terms
فاعل
حي عالم قادر قاصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidents can be independent causes of events.
Correction: Baqillani argues that accidents lack the necessary attributes of a true agent, so they cannot act as independent agents.

Checkpoint

A) Existence and eternity
B) Life, knowledge, power, and intention
C) Simplicity and immateriality
D) Necessity and contingency

Key Takeaway
Accidents cannot be agents because they lack the essential attributes of a true agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever unconsciously attribute power or effectiveness to created things (like your job, health, or technology) as if they were independent agents, forgetting that only the Living, Knowing, Powerful, Intending Creator truly acts? What would it take to shift that reliance to Allah alone?
?
When you find yourself trusting in natural causes or your own abilities without acknowledging Allah's agency behind them, how does that betray the truth that only a being with life, knowledge, power, and will can truly act? What step can you take today to make your reliance on Allah more conscious?
?
The passage says a true agent must be 'intending.' Have you ever mindlessly gone through acts of worship or daily tasks without directing your intention to Allah? How can you ensure that your actions are truly intended for Him, not just accidents of habit?

The Impossibility of Eternal Natures
Baqillani argues that eternal natures would make the world eternal, and his rebuttal to the naturalists' response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that if natures were eternal, the world would be eternal, and why the naturalists' response is untenable.

Core Idea
Eternal natures would necessitate an eternal world, and the causal direction is arbitrary.

Having shown that natures cannot be agents, Baqillani now tackles the possibility that these natures are eternal. He argues that if they were eternal, the world would also be eternal, and then questions why we should consider the nature as cause and the world as effect rather than the reverse.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional argument
Read the first part of the passage where Baqillani states the conditional: if natures were eternal, then the world would be eternal.
2
Understand the consequence
Note the reason: there is no impediment preventing the world from coexisting eternally with the nature.
3
Consider the counterargument
The naturalists might accept that the world is eternal. Baqillani anticipates this.
4
See the rebuttal
Baqillani argues that if both are eternal, then why is one considered cause and the other effect? The relationship is arbitrary.
This undermines their position.

Source Text

First, Baqillani reiterates that the four qualities are accidents, and accidents cannot perform actions because a true agent (fāʿil) must be living, knowing, powerful, and intending (especially for precise, well-ordered acts). If the naturalists counter that they accept the world is eternal, Baqillani retorts: if both nature and its effect are eternal, why is the nature considered the cause and the world the effect? The relationship could be reversed, or they could be independent. Thus, the naturalists' position is arbitrary and unsupported.

?
