The Mixing of Eternals and Temporal Composition
Baqillani uses a dialectical questioning to show the logical inconsistency in the naturalist claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's dialectical refutation of the claim that the world's composition is temporal while the natures are eternal.

Core Idea
If the natures are eternal, their mixing and thus the world's composition must also be eternal.

Baqillani confronts a key objection from naturalists: the world's composition is new, but the underlying natures are eternal. He uses a series of logical alternatives to show that this position leads to contradiction. Follow his reasoning step by step, even though the text is truncated due to a page break.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the dialectical structure: a series of questions and answers.
The text ends mid-sentence; the final branch is incomplete.
2
Identify the two main alternatives
Baqillani first asks whether the mixing of natures is identical to the natures themselves or a separate meaning. Locate these two branches.
3
Follow the consequences of each alternative
For each alternative, trace the logical outcome. If mixing is identical, composition is eternal. If separate, ask whether that meaning is eternal or temporal.
4
Recognize the truncated part
The argument stops at the question: 'Did it occur from a nature or not?' Consider what Baqillani might have concluded: if from a nature, infinite regress; if not, the world can be temporal without eternal natures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قالوا : العالَم مُحْدَث التركيب والتصوير عن اجتماع هذه الطبائع واختلاطها دون وجود ذواتها قيل لهم : فخبرونا عن اختلاط هذه الطبائع وامتزاجها : أهو هي أم معنى سواها ؟ فإن قالوا : هو هي قيل لهم : فهي قديمة الأعيان فيجب قدم تركيب العالم وتصويره لقدم الاختلاط، وإن قالوا : معنى سواها، قيل لهم : أقديم هو أم مُحدّث ؟ فإن قالوا : قديم قيل لهم : فيجب قِدَمُ التصوير والتركيب لقدم الاختلاط الموجب لذلك، وإن قالوا : مُحْدَث قيل لهم : أفمِنْ طبع حَدَث أم من غير طبع ؟ فإن قالوا : من طبع غير الطبائع
And if they say: The world is temporal in composition and formation from the coming together of these natures and their mixing, without their essences existing, it is said to them: Inform us about the mixing of these natures and their blending: is it identical to them or a meaning other than them? If they say: It is identical to them, it is said to them: Then they are eternal in essences, so the eternity of the composition and formation of the world is necessary due to the eternity of the mixing. And if they say: A meaning other than them, it is said to them: Is it eternal or temporal? If they say: Eternal, it is said to them: Then the eternity of formation and composition is necessary due to the eternity of the mixing that necessitates that. And if they say: Temporal, it is said to them: Did it occur from a nature or not from a nature? If they say: From a nature other than the natures...
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and a dialectical response. The naturalists claim that the world's composition (tarkīb) and formation (taṣwīr) are temporal (muḥdath), arising from the mixing of eternal natures, but the natures themselves do not exist as wholes (only their mixing). Baqillani asks: Is the mixing identical to the natures or something additional? If identical, then since the natures are eternal, the mixing is eternal, and thus the composition of the world must be eternal—contradicting temporality. If the mixing is an additional meaning (maʿnā), then it is either eternal or temporal. If eternal, again the composition is eternal. If temporal, then the mixing itself must have a cause: either from a nature or not. The text ends at this point, but the implication is that if it is from a nature, infinite regress follows, and if not, then the world can be temporal without a nature, reinforcing Baqillani's position.

تصوير
اختلاط
امتزاج
معنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mixing of natures is just the natures themselves, not an additional property.
Correction: Baqillani distinguishes between the essences of natures and the mixing of them, treating mixing as a distinct meaning (ma'na).

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani's response, if the mixing of natures is identical to the natures themselves, what follows?

B) The world's composition is eternal.
C) The mixing is an additional meaning.
D) The natures are not eternal.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani shows that whether mixing is identical to the natures or additional, if the natures are eternal, the world's composition must also be eternal—contradicting the claim of temporal composition.

Baqillani forces you to consider: if the mixing is temporal and not from a nature, then the natures are irrelevant. Causality and Created Things

The Four Primary Qualities Are Created Accidents
Baqillani proves the createdness of the four primary qualities by analogy to opposite accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the analogy of opposite accidents to prove that the four primary qualities (heat, cold, moisture, dryness) are created and not eternal.

Core Idea
Everything that changes must be created, and the four primary qualities change like all opposites.

Baqillani addresses a key argument of the naturalists who claimed that the world arises from eternal primary qualities. In this passage, he demonstrates that these qualities are actually accidents subject to change, just like motion and rest, and therefore must be originated. This argument serves as a linchpin for proving the entire world's contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four qualities
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the four qualities are heat (ḥarāra), cold (burūda), moisture (ruṭūba), and dryness (yubūsa).
These are referred to as 'ṭabā'iʿ' (natures) in the text.
2
Understand the analogy to motion and rest
Baqillani compares the four qualities to motion/rest and black/white. Ask: Why does he choose these examples? They are all opposite accidents that are known to be created.
The comparison establishes that the qualities are aʿrāḍ (accidents) and thus must be ḥāditha (created).
3
Grasp the logical impossibility of an eternal part
The author argues that an originated thing cannot be part of the eternal. Therefore, if the qualities were eternal universals (kulliyyāt), particular instances could not proceed from them. This refutes the idea of eternal natures.
The key phrase: 'al-muḥdath lā yajūzu an yakūna baʿḍan lil-qadīm'.
4
See the conclusion: these natures are created
Based on the argument, Baqillani judges that these ṭabā'iʿ are originated and that they are 'not natures' (ghayr ṭabāʾiʿ) in the sense of being eternal principles.
This directly refutes the naturalist claim.
5
Apply the argument to the whole world
The final sentence extends the logic: if the basic qualities can exist without an eternal nature, the entire world can also be originated without one.
This is a powerful generalization from the four qualities to all of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اليبوسة فهذه الطبائع جارية مجرى الحركة والسكون والسواد والبياض وسائر الأعراض المتضادة فيجب حدثها واستحالة كونها قديمة أو بعضا لكلية حرارة وبرودة ورطوبة ويبوسة قديمة أزلية لأن المحدث لا يجوز أن يكون بعضا للقديم كما لا يجوز أن تكون جزئيات الحركة والسكون عن كليات قديمة فوجب القضاء على حدوث هذه الطبائع وأنها غير طبائع فكذلك جائز حدوث سائر العالم عن غير طبيعة.
Dryness. So these natures follow the pattern of motion and rest, blackness and whiteness, and all other opposite accidents. Therefore their occurrence (huduth) is necessary, and it is impossible for them to be eternal or to be part of an eternal totality of heat, cold, moisture, and dryness that is eternal and pre-eternal. For what is originated (al-muhdath) cannot be a part of the eternal, just as it is not possible for particular instances of motion and rest to proceed from eternal universals. Thus it is necessary to judge that these natures are originated and that they are not natures. Similarly, it is permissible for the rest of the world to occur without a nature.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the four primary qualities (heat, cold, moisture, dryness) must be created (haditha). The author begins by comparing these qualities to other manifest opposites like motion/rest and black/white, which are known to be accidents (a'rad). Since these qualities are opposed and changeable, they cannot be eternal. The author then argues that if the qualities were eternal or part of an eternal universal (kulliya), then the individual instances (juz'iyyat) would be part of the eternal, which is impossible because what is originated cannot be a part of what is eternal. Therefore, these natures themselves are created, and they are not natures (tabai') that could be eternal. The conclusion extends to the rest of the world: if these basic qualities are created without a nature, then the entire world could also be created without requiring an eternal nature. This refutes the naturalist claim that the world arises from eternal inherent qualities.

Key Terms
طبائع (ṭabā'iʿ)
حدث (ḥudūth)
قديم (qadīm)
أعراض (aʿrāḍ)
كليات (kulliyyāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because heat, cold, etc. are always present in bodies, they must be essential or eternal.
Correction: Baqillani shows they are accidents subject to change, as they appear and disappear, and therefore must be created, not eternal.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani compare the four primary qualities to motion and rest?
A) To show they are similar in being opposite accidents.
B) To prove they are eternal.
C) To argue they are substances.
D) To deny they exist.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani proves that the four primary qualities are created accidents, refuting the view that they are eternal natures and thereby supporting the contingency of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the most basic qualities of the world around you—heat, cold, moisture, dryness—are created and sustained by Allah every moment, how does that change the way you perceive your daily physical comforts and discomforts? Will you let this truth turn your attention to the Creator whenever you feel a breeze or a sunbeam?
?
Baqillani argues that what is originated cannot be part of the eternal. Your own existence is originated. Are you living as if your life is a part of something eternal and self-sufficient, or do you fully acknowledge that you are a contingent being, completely dependent on Allah's constant creation? What would change if you truly internalized this?
?
The proof ends by saying the world can occur without a nature—meaning it is not driven by inherent forces but by Allah's direct will. Is there an area of your life where you are relying on 'natural causes' (your habits, your strength, your plans) rather than actively seeing them as channels of Allah's immediate power? What one step can you take today to shift that reliance?

Accidents Cannot Act as Agents
Baqillani argues that accidents cannot act as agents because an agent must be alive, knowing, powerful, and willing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why accidents cannot serve as agents that produce the world, and what attributes a true agent must possess.

Core Idea
Only a living, knowing, powerful, and willing being can be a true agent.

Imam Baqillani continues his refutation of naturalists by arguing that the four primary qualities (heat, cold, etc.) are accidents. Since accidents cannot act as agents—because a genuine agent must be alive, knowing, powerful, and intending—these natures cannot be the cause of the world. This argument establishes that the world's precise order demands a personal Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of Baqillani's concise argument.
Pay attention to the key terms like 'fā'il' and 'a'rād'.
2
Identify the agent's conditions
Find the four attributes that Baqillani says a true agent must have: alive, knowing, powerful, and intending.
The phrase 'حيا عالما قادرا قاصدا' lists them directly.
3
Understand why accidents fail
Consider why accidents cannot satisfy these conditions—because they are not living, knowing, etc. Therefore, they cannot be agents.
This refutes the naturalist position that natures can act alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى أنا قد بينا أن هذه الأجناس أعراض وبينا أن الأعراض لا يجوز أن تفعل شيئا لأن الفاعل لا يكون إلا حيا عالما قادرا قاصدا إذا كان فعله محكما فلم يجز أن تكون هذه الطبائع فاعلة للعالم.
And we have already shown that these kinds are accidents, and we have shown that accidents are not permitted to do anything, because the agent (al-fā'il) is only alive, knowing, powerful, and intending when his action is precise. So it is not permissible for these natures to be agents of the world.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument: the four primary qualities are accidents (a'rād), and accidents cannot act as agents. The author states that a true agent (fā'il) must possess four attributes: life (hayy), knowledge ('ālim), power (qādir), and will (qāsid). Additionally, the agent's action must be precise (muhkam). Since the four qualities are accidents and lack these attributes, they cannot be the agents that produce the world. This refutes the naturalist claim that the world arises spontaneously from these natures.

Key Terms
أعراض (a'rād)
فاعل (fā'il)
حي (hayy)
عالم ('ālim)
قادر (qādir)
قاصد (qāsid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natural forces like heat and cold can act independently as agents that produce order.
Correction: Baqillani argues that a true agent must be alive, knowing, powerful, and intending. Accidents lack these attributes, so they cannot be agents.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what conditions must an agent fulfill for his action to be precise?
A) He must be eternal and unchanging.
B) He must be alive, knowing, powerful, and intending.
C) He must be a substance, not an accident.
D) He must be the first cause.

Key Takeaway
Accidents lack the necessary attributes to be agents; only a living, knowing, powerful, and willing being can act precisely as an agent.

?
The author says a true agent must be intending. When you consider your own intentions, are they directed towards pleasing the only true Agent? Reflect on a recent action and ask yourself: was my intention aligned with acknowledging that all agency belongs to Allah? Write down one intention you will purify today.

Baqillani's Argument Against Eternal Natures
Baqillani disproves the eternity of the four natures via a conditional proof and a reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that if the four natures were eternal, the world would be eternal alongside them, and why this disproves the eternity of natures.

Core Idea
An eternal cause without impediment necessitates an eternal effect.

Baqillani presents a concise yet powerful proof against the eternity of the four natures. He argues that if the natures were eternal, the world would have to be eternal too, because there is no barrier preventing it. He then tackles an anticipated objection, exposing an absurd consequence. This lesson walks through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Read the first part of the passage: 'if they were eternal... then its eternity would be necessary along with their eternity'. Understand the conditional logic.
Notice the key phrase 'لا مانع يمنع' (no impediment preventing).
2
Identify the role of impediment
Reflect on why Baqillani mentions 'لا مانع'. In kalam, an impediment is what prevents an eternal cause from producing an eternal effect. Here, there is none.
3
Examine the objection and response
Read the second part: the opponents might accept that the world is eternal too. Baqillani then asks why the nature is the cause and not the effect, showing the arbitrariness.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Connect to the broader context
Understand that this argument fits within a set of proofs for the temporal origination of the world. It refutes the claim that natures are eternal causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل على استحالة قدم هذه الطبائع أنها لو كانت قديمة، وكان العالم حادثا عنها، لوجب قدمه مع قدمها على ما بيناه من قبل إذ لا مانع يمنع من كونه معها. فإن قالوا : كذلك نقول قيل لهم : فإذا كان الطبع قديما أزليا، وكان الكائن عنه قديما أزليا، فلم كان أحدهما بأن يكون موجبا للآخر وسببا له أن يكون المسبب سببا وعلة؟ فلا يجدون في ذلك متعلقا.
And among what indicates the impossibility of the eternity of these natures is that if they were eternal, and the world was originated from them, then its eternity would be necessary along with their eternity, as we have explained before, since there is no impediment preventing it from being with them. If they say: 'We say the same,' it is said to them: 'So if the nature is eternal and pre-eternal, and that which comes from it is eternal and pre-eternal, then why is one of them more deserving to be the cause of the other and a reason for it, such that the effect is a cause and a reason?' They will not find any basis in that.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the four natures cannot be eternal. The argument is: if these natures were eternal and the world originated from them, then the world would have to be eternal along with them, because there is no barrier (māni') to prevent its coexistence with them from eternity. The author anticipates an objection: the opponents might say they accept that (i.e., they admit the world is also eternal). The author responds by pointing out that if both the nature and its effect are eternal, then there is no reason to make one the cause (musabbib) and the other the effect (sabab, 'illa). The relationship of causality becomes arbitrary. Since the opponents cannot justify why the nature is the cause rather than the effect, their position is baseless. Thus the eternity of the natures is impossible.

Key Terms
قدم (qidam)
قديم (qadīm)
أزلي (azalī)
موجب (mūjib)
سبب (sabab)
علة ('illa)
مانع (māni')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An eternal cause can produce a temporal effect even if there is no impediment.
Correction: Baqillani argues that if a cause is eternal and has no impediment, its effect must also be eternal. A temporal effect would require an impediment or change in the cause.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, if the four natures were eternal and the world originated from them, what would necessarily follow?
A) The world would be originated.
B) The world would be eternal alongside the natures.
C) The natures would cease to exist.
D) The world would have an impediment.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's argument shows that if an eternal cause has no impediment, its effect must be eternal, and thus the four natures cannot be eternal causes of a temporal world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believe in an eternal Creator, does this argument challenge you to reconsider how you think about God's relationship with creation? Are there ways you unconsciously imagine God and the world as co-eternal, even if you reject it intellectually? What can you do today to purify your tawhid from such subtle associations?
?
The opponents had no basis to distinguish cause and effect when both are eternal. Where in your own life have you assumed causality without evidence, simply because something has always been the case? Will you commit to examining your assumptions this week?
?
This argument relies on the necessity of an eternal effect from an eternal cause without impediment. Do you ever treat God's attributes as if they operate under the same necessity, forgetting that God's will is free? How does this affect your relationship with Him, and what will you change about your reliance on Him?

The Dilemma of Mixture and the Fifth Nature
Baqillani examines the claim that the world's composition results from mixture of the four natures, leading to a logical dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma Baqillani uses to refute the claim that the world's composition arises from the mixture of eternal natures.

Core Idea
Mixture is either eternal or caused; either way, the opponent's position collapses.

In this passage, Baqillani systematically dismantles the argument that the world's intricate composition results from the mixture of the four eternal natures. He shows that this claim leads to inescapable contradictions, forcing the opponent either to admit a fifth nature or to concede that all things could arise without any nature at all.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the logical flow. Notice how Baqillani poses questions that branch into multiple possibilities.
The argument is a classic dilemma: each branch leads to a conclusion that contradicts the original claim.
2
Trace the branches of the dilemma
Identify the first question: Is the mixture identical to the natures or a separate meaning? Then follow the branches for each answer. Write down the consequences of each path.
If mixture is identical to the natures, it is eternal, so the world's composition would be eternal. If it is separate, then it must be either eternal or originated.
3
See how both branches refute the opponent
Notice that whether the mixture is eternal or originated, the opponent ends up with a contradiction: either the world is eternal (if mixture eternal) or they must admit a fifth nature or that no nature is needed at all. In every case, their original position is refuted.
The power of the argument is that it leaves the opponent with no way out.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قالوا : العالم محدث التركيب والتصوير عن اجتماع هذه الطبائع واختلاطها دون وجود ذواتها قيل لهم : فخبرونا عن اختلاط هذه الطبائع وامتزاجها : أهو هي أم معنى سواها ؟ فإن قالوا : هو هي قيل لهم : فهي قديمة الأعيان فيجب قدم تركيب العالم وتصويره لقدم الاختلاط، وإن قالوا : معنى سواها، قيل : أقديم هو أم محدث ؟ فإن قالوا : قديم قيل لهم : فيجب قدم التصوير والتركيب لقدم الاختلاط الموجب لذلك، وإن قالوا : محدث قيل لهم : أفمن طبع حدث أم من غير طبع ؟ فإن قالوا : من طبع غير الطبائع الأربع، أقروا بطبع خامس وتركوا قولهم ؛ وإن قالوا بغير طبع، قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم من جواز حدوث تركيب العالم وسائر الأشكال بغير طبع؟ فلا يجدون ذلك مخرجا.
And if they say: 'The world is originated in its composition and form from the meeting of these natures and their mixture, without the existence of their essences,' it is said to them: 'Inform us about the mixture of these natures and their blending: is it they themselves or a meaning other than them?' If they say: 'It is they themselves,' it is said to them: 'Then they are eternal in their essences, so the eternity of the world's composition and form is necessary due to the eternity of the mixture.' And if they say: 'A meaning other than them,' it is said: 'Is it eternal or originated?' If they say: 'Eternal,' it is said to them: 'Then the eternity of the form and composition is necessary due to the eternity of the mixture that necessitates that.' And if they say: 'Originated,' it is said to them: 'Is it from an originated nature or from without a nature?' If they say: 'From a nature other than the four natures,' they have admitted a fifth nature and abandoned their claim. And if they say: 'Without a nature,' it is said to them: 'Then what prevents you from allowing the occurrence of the composition of the world and all forms without a nature?' They will not find any way out of that.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed dilemma concerning the claim that the world's composition and form arise from the mixture of the four natures without their essences existing. The author poses a series of questions that force the opponent into contradictions. First, is the mixture identical to the natures or something else? If identical, then since the natures are eternal, the mixture is eternal, and hence the world's composition would be eternal, contradicting its originatedness. If the mixture is something else, then it must be either eternal or originated. If eternal, the composition is again eternal. If originated, then it must originate either from a nature or without a nature. If from a nature other than the four, that admits a fifth nature, which the opponent denied. If without a nature, then why cannot the whole world be originated without a nature? Thus the opponent is left with no escape.

Key Terms
تركيب (tarkīb)
تصوير (taswīr)
اجتماع (ijtimā')
اختلاط (ikhtilāt)
امتزاج (imtizāj)
معنى (ma'nā)
طبع خامس (tab' khāmis)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that mixture of eternal elements can explain the originated forms of the world without contradiction.
Correction: Baqillani shows that mixture itself must be either eternal or caused. If eternal, the forms become eternal. If caused, the cause must be either a fifth nature or no nature at all, both of which undermine the original claim.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the mixture of the four natures is identical to the natures themselves?
A) The mixture is originated.
B) The world's composition is eternal.
C) The natures are created.
D) There is no consequence.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's dilemma forces the opponent to admit that their claim leads either to an eternal world, a fifth nature, or the possibility of creation without any nature—all of which refute their original position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the intricate order of the world, do you ever assume it just 'naturally' came to be without a cause? Baqillani's argument forces you to consider that every mixture or composition must have an origin. How does this challenge the way you think about the necessity of a Creator in your own life? Will you now pause and see every orderly thing as a sign of intentional design, rather than mere chance?
?
If you were to argue that the universe's complexity arose from the combination of simple elements, Baqillani demands you explain the source of that combination. Look at your own habits and assumptions: where in your daily life do you attribute things to 'just happening' without considering a deeper cause? How can you cultivate a mindset that always asks, 'What caused this?' until you reach the Creator?
?
Baqillani's dilemma shows that refusing to admit a fifth nature leads to the possibility of creation without any nature at all. This strips away all excuses for denying a transcendent origin. Are you willing to let go of every intellectual crutch that makes you comfortable with a world without a Creator? What specific belief or assumption are you holding onto that this argument challenges, and what will you do about it?

The Impossibility of Spontaneous Combination of Opposites
Baqillani refutes spontaneous combination of the four natures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the claim that the four contrary natures could combine spontaneously without an external unifier.

Core Idea
Opposites cannot unite without a unifier; to claim otherwise is absurd.

This lesson presents a classic kalam argument from Imam al-Baqillani against the naturalist claim that the four basic natures (fire, air, water, earth) combined on their own to form the world. Baqillani shows that these natures are inherently repulsive and cannot come together without an external force. By reading this passage, you will see how he forces the opponent into a dilemma: either admit a Maker, contradicting their own materialism, or accept an absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage Carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Baqillani describes the four natures as mutually repelling and separate.
The passage uses strong language like 'mutanāfira' (mutually repelling) and 'mutabāyina' (separate).
2
Understand the Nature of the Four Elements
Baqillani emphasizes that each nature has its own place and tends to avoid the others. Consider the examples of heavy (thaqīl) and light (khafīf) – they naturally move in opposite directions.
Heavy things fall, light things rise; they do not mix spontaneously.
3
Follow the Dilemma
Baqillani presents a dilemma: either the opponent admits an external cause (a maker or another nature) that forces combination, or they must accept that combination happens spontaneously. The first option contradicts their original claim that the world arises from the four natures alone; the second is absurd.
If they admit a maker, they have abandoned their materialism. If they deny, they must accept impossible outcomes.
4
Consider the Implications
Reflect on why this argument is effective. It shows that the materialist explanation of the world's origin is logically incoherent because it cannot account for the combination of opposites without an external agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم : كيف اجتمعت هذه الطبائع الأربع وتركبت في الأجسام وهي لم تزل متنافرة متباينة حيز كل واحد في القدم غير حيز صاحبه وطبع كل شيء منها البعد عن صاحبه والنفور عنه ؟ وهل يجوز أن يجتمع شيئان أحدهما ثقيل يهوى وينزل بطبعه والآخر خفيف متصاعد بطبعه من غير جامع يجمعهما وقامع يقمعهما على الاجتماع لأن ما هذا سبيله متى لم يقهر على الاجتماع لم يزد من الاجتماع والتقارب إلا بعدا؟ فإن قالوا : ها هنا صانع أو طبيعة أخرى قهرت هذه الطبائع على الاختلاط والاجتماع بعد التنافر والتباعد والتضاد، تركوا قولهم وأثبتوا طبعا خامسا وصانعا غيرها ؛ وإن أجازوا ذلك بغير صانع ألزموا اجتماع الخفيف والثقيل، والمنحدر والمتصاعد بغير سبب ولا جامع بل بسجيتهما وسوم أنفسهما ولا فصل في ذلك.
And it is said to them: 'How did these four natures come together and combine in bodies, while they have never ceased to be mutually repelling, separate, the place (hayyiz) of each one in pre-eternity being other than the place of its companion, and the nature (tab') of each one of them is distance from its companion and aversion from it? And is it permissible for two things, one heavy that falls and descends by its nature and the other light that rises by its nature, to come together without a gatherer (jāmi') that gathers them and a suppressor (qāmi') that suppresses them to union? For such things, when they are not forced to union, only increase in distance and remoteness? If they say: 'Here is a maker (ṣāni') or another nature that forced these natures to mixture and union after repulsion, separation, and opposition,' they have abandoned their claim and affirmed a fifth nature and a maker other than them. And if they allow that without a maker, they are forced to admit the union of the light and heavy, the descending and ascending, without cause or gatherer, but merely by their own natures and their own volition, and there is no distinction in that.'
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage argues from the inherent opposition of the four natures. They are described as mutually repelling (mutanāfira), separate (mutabāyina), each having its own spatial location in pre-eternity, and naturally inclined to distance themselves from each other. The author asks how such contrary properties (heavy vs. light) could ever combine without an external force. If the opponent admits there is a maker (ṣāni') or another nature that forced them together, then they contradict their own position by positing a fifth nature or an external maker. If they claim the combination occurs spontaneously without any cause, they are forced to accept absurdities like heavy and light coming together by their own nature and will, which is impossible. Thus the original claim that the world arises from the mixture of these four natures without an external agent is refuted.

Key Terms
متنافرة (mutanāfira)
متباينة (mutabāyina)
حيز (hayyiz)
ثقيل (thaqīl)
خفيف (khafīf)
جامع (jāmi')
قامع (qāmi')
صانع (ṣāni')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natural opposites can spontaneously combine to form complex structures without any external cause.
Correction: Baqillani shows that opposites like heavy and light only repel each other; they cannot come together without a unifier. Thus the materialist explanation of the world's origin is logically flawed.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the opponent claims that the four natures combine without any external force?
A) The natures would be eternal.
B) They must admit a fifth nature or a maker.
C) They would have to accept that opposites combine spontaneously, which is absurd.
D) The world would be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The four contrary natures cannot combine without an external unifier; thus the materialist explanation of the world's origin is logically incoherent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, where do you see opposing tendencies (such as your desires for comfort and for striving) that only come together through a higher purpose? What 'unifier' are you relying on to hold them together, and will you consciously submit to it?
?
If the natural world cannot order itself without a Maker, what does that say about the chaos inside your own heart? What external, divine force are you allowing to suppress your disordered passions, or are you still trying to let them spontaneously sort themselves out?
?
The naturalists denied the need for a unifier and ended in absurdity. In what area of your beliefs are you trying to combine opposites (like belief in your own self-sufficiency alongside trust in God) without a reconciling truth? Will you admit the absurdity and accept the only true unifier?

Refuting the Inseparability Argument for Composition
Baqillani refutes the composition argument by a reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the argument that bodies are composed of the four natures based on inseparability, by showing it leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
If inseparability meant composition, bodies would be made of everything.

Baqillani now takes on a key argument from naturalists who claim that because the four primary qualities are never absent from bodies, they must be the building blocks of bodies. He shows this logic is too broad — if we follow it, bodies would also be composed of light, colors, tastes, and other continuously present accidents. This reductio ad absurdum exposes the flaw in their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the original argument
The naturalists reason: bodies are never found without the four natures, therefore bodies are composed of them. Grasp this premise and conclusion.
2
Notice Baqillani's counter
He applies the same logic to other inseparable attributes (light, colors, etc.) and says it would force them to say bodies are composed of all those things.
The list includes: light, darkness, colors, tastes, smells, motions, rests, and everything inseparable.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Since it is absurd to say bodies are composed of all these accidents, the original argument is invalid.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما اعتلالهم بأنهم لم يجدوا جسما يخلو من هذه الطبائع الأربع فوجب أن تكون الأجسام مركبة منها فإنه يوجب عليهم أن تكون الأجسام مركبة من النور والظلام والألوان والطعوم والروائح والحركات والسكنات وسائر ما لا تنفك منه الأجسام وفي بطلان ذلك دليل على بطلان ما قالوا.
And as for their reasoning that they did not find a body free from these four natures, so bodies must be composed of them, it would require them to hold that bodies are composed of light and darkness, colors, tastes, smells, motions, rests, and everything from which bodies are inseparable. And in the falsity of that is evidence for the falsity of what they said.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage attacks an argument by the naturalists that because no body is ever found devoid of the four primary qualities (heat, cold, moisture, dryness), those qualities must be the constituent components of bodies. The author points out that this reasoning can be extended to many other inseparable attributes of bodies, such as light and darkness, colors, tastes, smells, motion and rest, and so on. If inseparability were a valid proof of composition, then bodies would be composed of all these disparate attributes, which is patently false. Therefore, the original argument that the four natures are the material components of bodies is also false.

Key Terms
اعتلال (i'tilāl)
مركبة (murakkaba)
نور (nūr)
ظلام (zulām)
ألوان (alwān)
طعوم (ṭu'ūm)
روائح (rawā'iḥ)
حركات (ḥarakāt)
سكنات (sakanāt)

Misconception: If an attribute is always present in a body, that attribute must be a constituent part of the body.
Correction: Baqillani shows this reasoning is flawed because it would entail that bodies are composed of many inseparable accidents (light, color, etc.), which is absurd.

Checkpoint

A) Bodies are simple substances.
B) The same reasoning would make bodies composed of countless other inseparable accidents.
C) The four natures are not always present.
D) Bodies are not real.

Key Takeaway

Refuting the Materialist Four Natures Argument
Baqillani forces materialists to choose between a creator and absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the inherent repulsion of the four natures to force materialists into admitting a transcendent creator or an absurdity.

Core Idea
Opposite natures cannot unite spontaneously—only a creator can compel them.

Baqillani presents a powerful dialectical argument against materialists who claim that the four basic natures (hot, cold, wet, dry) combine on their own in bodies. He points out that these natures are inherently repelling, like heavy and light. Their union requires an external force. The materialist is cornered: either admit a creator or accept an impossible self-union of opposites.

Guided Path
1
Understand the materialist claim
Recognize that materialists argue the four natures combine spontaneously in bodies without a creator.
This is the starting point of the argument.
2
Read Baqillani's dilemma
The natures are repelling (e.g., heavy vs. light), so they cannot unite without an external gatherer.
3
Examine the two forced outcomes
If the opponent says a creator or a fifth nature compelled them, they abandon materialism. If they deny any external cause, they must accept the absurdity that opposites unite by themselves.
4
Recognize the dialectical method
Baqillani does not endorse the four natures; he uses the opponent's framework to refute them.
This is a common tactic in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم : كيف اجتمعت هذه الطبائع الأربع وتركبت في الأجسام وهي لم تزل متنافرة متباينة حيز كل واحد في القدم غير حيز صاحبه وطبع كل شيء منها البعد عن صاحبه والنفور عنه؟ وهل يجوز أن يجتمع شيئان أحدهما ثقيل يهوى وينزل بطبعه والآخر خفيف متصاعد بطبعه من غير جامع يجمعهما وقامع يقمعهما على الاجتماع لأن ما هذا سبيله متى لم يقهر على الاجتماع لم يزدد من الاجتماع والتقارب إلا بعدا؟ فإن قالوا ها هنا صانع أو طبيعة قهرت هذه الطبائع على الاختلاط والاجتماع بعد التنافر والتباعد والتضاد تركوا قولهم وأثبتوا طبعا خامسا وصانعا غيرها وإن أجازوا ذلك بغير صانع الزموا اجتماع الخفيف والثقيل والمنحدر والمتصاعد بغير سبب ولا جامع بل بسجيتهما وسوم أنفسهما ولا فصل في ذلك.
And it is said to them: How did these four natures come together and combine in bodies, while they have never ceased to be mutually repelling, distinct, each one's place in pre-eternity being other than its companion's place, and the nature of each thing among them is distance from its companion and aversion from it? And is it permissible that two things, one of them heavy, falling and descending by its nature, and the other light, ascending by its nature, come together without a gatherer that gathers them and a subduer that subdues them to union? Because whatever is in this state, when it is not forced into union, only increases in distance and separation? If they say: here there is a Maker or a nature that compelled these natures to mix and come together after repulsion, divergence, and opposition, they have abandoned their statement and established a fifth nature and a Maker other than them. And if they permit that without a Maker, they are compelled [to accept] the coming together of the light and the heavy, the descending and the ascending, without cause or gatherer, but rather by their own innate disposition and of themselves—and there is no distinction [that prevents this].
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer refutation of the materialist claim that the four natures (hot, cold, wet, dry) combine spontaneously in bodies. The author (Baqillani) presents the dilemma: these natures are inherently repelling each other (heavy vs. light, descending vs. ascending). They cannot unite without an external force. If the materialist says a creator or a fifth nature forced them together, they have abandoned pure materialism and admitted a transcendent maker. If they say no external force is needed, they are forced to accept the absurdity that opposites unite without any cause. Thus the argument refutes the materialist position on its own terms.

Key Terms
طبائع
صانع
اجتماع
تنافر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Baqillani assumes the four natures are real entities.
Correction: This is a dialectical argument that works within the opponent's framework; Baqillani does not endorse the four natures as real.

Checkpoint
What is the key dilemma Baqillani poses to those who claim bodies are composed of four natures?
A) The natures cannot combine because they are all heavy.
B) The natures are repelling, so they need an external agent to unite—leading to admission of a creator.
C) The natures are identical, so no combination is needed.
D) The natures are eternal, so they have always been combined.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's argument forces materialists to either admit a transcendent creator or accept an absurd self-union of opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the order in your own life, do you subconsciously attribute it to 'natural causes' (luck, effort, circumstance) without recognizing the Hand of the One who compels them to work? Today, before you credit any outcome to a 'system', pause and say: 'O Compeller, You alone sustain this.'
?
Have you ever, like the materialist, mentally excluded Allah from a cause-and-effect chain in your daily affairs—thinking 'this happened because of that' without linking it to the Creator? Commit to verbally affirming, 'This is by Allah's decree and power,' for every occurrence today.
?
The materialist's absurdity is to believe opposites unite on their own. But what about the opposites in your own soul—desire for sin and love of Allah? Do you think you can harmonize them without a divine Compeller? Confess your need for Allah to subdue your lower self every moment.

Refuting the Argument from Inseparability of Natures
Baqillani refutes the claim that bodies must be composed of four natures because they are never found without them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the argument that bodies must be composed of four natures by showing its logical consequences lead to absurdity.

Core Idea
Inseparability does not imply composition; otherwise bodies would be composed of everything inseparable.

Imam Baqillani responds to a materialist argument for the composition of bodies from four natures. The materialists claim that because bodies are never found without these natures, the natures must be constituents. Baqillani exposes the logical flaw by showing that the same reasoning would force absurd conclusions. Let's examine his concise refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then the literal translation. Try to grasp the flow of the argument before analyzing it.
Notice the key term 'اعتلال' which means rationalization or excuse.
2
Identify the materialist claim
The materialists claim: 'We have not found a body devoid of the four natures, therefore bodies are composed of them.' Identify this premise and conclusion in the text.
3
Follow Baqillani's parity argument
Baqillani applies the same reasoning to other inseparable attributes like light, darkness, colors, etc. See how he extends the logic to show its absurdity.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: if the reasoning were sound, it would prove too much.
4
Understand the logical structure
Recognize that Baqillani is not denying that bodies have these attributes, but rejecting the inference from 'always present' to 'constituent part.'
This exposes a fallacy of composition.
5
Memorize key terms
Write down the Arabic terms: اعتلال, مركبة, انفكاك, طبائع. Understand their meanings from the context.
These terms are central to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما اعتلالهم بأنهم لم يجدوا جسما يخلو من هذه الطبائع الأربع فوجب أن تكون الأجسام مركبة منها فإنه يوجب عليهم أن تكون الأجسام مركبة من النور والظلام والألوان والطعوم والروائح والحركات والسكنات وسائر ما لا تنفك منه الأجسام وفي بطلان ذلك دليل على بطلان ما قالوا.
As for their rationalization that they have not found a body devoid of these four natures, so it follows that bodies are composed of them—this would require of them that bodies be composed of light and darkness, colors, tastes, odors, motions and states of rest, and all other things from which bodies are not free. And in the falsity of that is evidence for the falsity of what they said.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific materialist proof. The materialists argue: since we never observe a body without the four natures (hot, cold, wet, dry), bodies must be composed of them. Baqillani shows this reasoning is invalid by parity: if that argument were sound, bodies would also have to be composed of many other inseparable attributes like light, darkness, colors, etc., because bodies are never found without those either. Since it is absurd to say bodies are composed of all those attributes, the original argument is unsound. This exposes a logical flaw in the materialist inference.

Key Terms
اعتلال
مركبة
انفكاك
طبائع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because bodies are never found without the four natures, the natures must be constituent parts of bodies.
Correction: Baqillani shows that this reasoning is invalid because the same logic would force the absurd conclusion that bodies are composed of many other inseparable attributes (like colors and motions) which is clearly false.

Checkpoint
What logical error does Baqillani highlight in the materialist argument from inseparability?
A) They assume that what is always present must be a part, but that would absurdly imply composition from many other things like colors.
B) They ignore that bodies can exist without natures in some cases.
C) They confuse essence with accident.
D) They rely on empirical observation which is unreliable.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the materialist argument by showing that the reasoning from inseparability to composition leads to absurdity when applied consistently to other inseparable attributes.

?

?
This lesson shows that consistency in reasoning can overturn a seemingly obvious conclusion. What argument have you accepted without testing its consistency across all cases? Will you go back and examine it today?

Baqillani's Refutation of Natures as Efficient Causes
Baqillani argues that if natures cause effects like burning, they must be either the body itself or an accident—both impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that natures cannot be independent causes because they must be either bodies or accidents, both of which are impossible.

Core Idea
If natures cause effects, they must be either bodies or accidents—both options fail.

Imam Baqillani takes on the materialist claim that natures (like the nature of fire) are real causes of effects such as burning. He systematically shows that this view leads to contradictions. In this passage, he considers two possible ways a nature could exist—as the body itself or as an accident—and refutes both. His argument uses logical consistency and observed differences in the world.

Guided Path
1
Understand the materialist claim
Start by recognising the claim: that natures (e.g., the nature of fire) are active causes that produce effects like burning. Baqillani sets out to refute this.
2
Examine the first possibility: nature as the body
Read the argument: if the nature is the body itself, then all bodies of the same genus should produce the same effects. But we observe differences (e.g., not all bodies burn), so this is false.
The key principle: similar causes produce similar effects.
3
Examine the second possibility: nature as an accident
Read the refutation: if the nature is an accident (a quality), then it cannot act because accidents are passive. This is a basic principle in kalam.
Accidents like color cannot cause effects; only substances can.
4
Conclude the refutation
Since both possibilities are impossible, the theory of natures as efficient causes is invalid. The true cause is Allah.
This does not deny habitual causation, only independent causal power to natures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل أيضا على فساد ما يذهبون إليه من إثبات فعل الطبائع أنه لو كان الإسكار والإحراق والتبريد والتسخين والشبع والري وغير ذلك الأمر الحادثة واقعة عن طبيعة من الطبائع لكان ذلك الطبع لا يخلو من أن يكون هو نفس الجسم المطبوع أو معنى سواه؛ فإن كان هو نفس الجسم أن يكون تناول سائر الأجسام يوجب حدوث الإسكار والشبع والري ومجاورة كل جسم يوجب التبريد والتسخين لقيام الدليل على أن الأجسام كلها من جنس واحد وقد علم أن الشيء إذا أوجب أمرا ما وأثر تأثيرا ما وجب أن يكون ما هو مثله وما جانسه موجبا لمثل حكمه وتأثيره كالسوادين الموجودين بالمحل والحركتين في الجهة الواحدة وما جرى مجراها من الأجناس وفي العلم باختلاف ما يحدث عند تناول هذه الأجسام دليل على أنه لا يجوز أن يكون الموجب لشيء منه بعض الأجسام الذي هو مجانس لسائرها وأنه الشبع والري والإسكار لو وجبت عن تناول الطعام والشراب لوجب حدوث ذلك عند تناول الحصى والتراب والفث والحنظل وأن يحدث الري عند شرب الخل والبلسان وسائر المائعات والجامدات أيضا لأنها والإسكار من جنس الطعام والشراب. وإن كان ذلك الطبع الذي يومئون إليه عرضا من الأعراض فسد إثباته من وجوه: أحدها أن الأعراض لا يجوز أن تكون فاعلة كما لا يجوز أن تفعل الأفعال الألوان والأكوان وغيرهما من أجناس الأعراض وكما لا يجوز أن
And among what also indicates the corruption of what they adhere to in affirming the action of natures is that if intoxication, burning, cooling, heating, satiety, thirst-quenching, and other occurring events come about from a nature among natures, then that nature cannot but be either the very body possessing the nature or a meaning other than it. If it is the very body, then partaking of all bodies would necessitate the occurrence of intoxication, satiety, and thirst-quenching, and proximity to every body would necessitate cooling and heating, because the proof has been established that all bodies are of one genus, and it is known that when a thing necessitates something and produces an effect, what is like it and of its kind must necessitate a similar judgment and effect—like the two blacknesses present in a locus, and the two motions in one direction, and whatever else runs their course among genera. And in our knowledge of the difference in what occurs when partaking of these bodies is evidence that it is not permissible that the necessitator of any of that be some bodies which are of the same genus as the rest. And that if satiety, thirst-quenching, and intoxication were necessitated by partaking of food and drink, then it would be necessary for that to occur when partaking of pebbles, dust, futh, and colocynth, and that thirst-quenching would occur when drinking vinegar, balsam, and all other liquids and solids as well, because they and intoxication are of the same genus as food and drink. And if that nature to which they refer is an accident among accidents, then its affirmation is invalid from several aspects: one of them is that accidents cannot be agents, just as colors, accidents of location, and other genera of accidents cannot perform actions, and just as it is not permissible that...
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a two-part refutation of the materialist claim that natures (taba'i') are active causes (e.g., fire burns by its nature). The author considers two possibilities: either the nature is the body itself, or it is an accident (attribute) added to the body. He refutes both. First, if the nature is the body itself, then since all bodies are of one genus, all bodies should produce the same effects (e.g., all food should intoxicate, all bodies should burn when touched). But we observe differences, so this is false. Second, if the nature is an accident, then accidents cannot be agents (fa'ila) because they are passive; only substances can act. Thus the theory of natures as efficient causes is invalid. The argument uses the principle of uniformity of genus and the distinction between substance and accident.

Key Terms
طبائع
إسكار
إحراق
عرض
فاعلية
جنس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Baqillani denies all natural causality, including the habitual relationship between fire and burning.
Correction: Baqillani only denies that natures have independent causal power; he affirms that God creates effects habitually through causes, but the causes themselves are not agents.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Baqillani's argument against the idea that a nature (like the nature of fire) causes burning?
A) Fire does not exist, so it cannot cause burning.
B) If the nature is the body, all similar bodies would have the same effect, but they differ; if it is an accident, accidents cannot cause effects.
C) Burning is an illusion caused by the mind.
D) Natures are eternal and uncreated, so they cannot be causes.

Key Takeaway
Natures cannot be independent causes because they would have to be either bodies or accidents, both of which are impossible—this refutes the materialist theory of causality.

Refutation of Natures as Identical to Bodies
Baqillani refutes the idea that natures are identical to bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's logical argument against the claim that natures are identical to the bodies they inhabit.

Core Idea
If natures were identical to bodies, all similar bodies would cause identical effects, which they do not.

This passage presents a key argument from Imam Baqillani against those who claim that natural phenomena are caused by inherent natures. He focuses on the logical consequence of identifying the nature with the body itself. We'll walk through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Consider the hypothesis
Baqillani sets up a hypothetical: if a nature were identical to the body, then all bodies of the same genus should produce identical effects.
This is a reductio ad absurdum strategy.
2
Examine the principle of homogeneity
He invokes the principle that like causes produce like effects, using examples like two blacknesses or two motions in the same direction.
3
Observe the contradiction
We know from experience that different bodies produce different effects (e.g., food satiates but stones do not), which contradicts the hypothesis.
Thus the nature cannot be identical to the body itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل أيضاً على فساد ما يذهبون إليه من إثبات فعل الطبائع أنه لو كان الإسكار والإحراق والتبريد والتسخين والشبع والري وغير ذلك (۱) الأمور الحادثة واقعةً (۲) عن طبيعة من الطبائع لكان ذلك الطبع لا يخلو من (۳) أن يكون هو نفس الجسم المطبوع أو معنى سواه ؛ فإن كان هو نفس الجسم أن يكون تَناوُلُ سائر الأجسام يوجب حدوث الإسكار والشبع والري ومجاورة كل جسم يوجب التبريد والتسخين لقيام الدليل على أن الأجسام كلّها من جنس واحد وقد عُلم أن الشيء إذا أوجب أمراً ما وأثر تأثيراً ما وجب أن يكون ما هو مثله وما جانسه موجباً لمثل حكمه، وتأثيره، كالسوادين الموجودين بالمحلّ، والحركتين في الجهة الواحدة وما جرى مجراها من الأجناس وفي العلم باختلاف ما يحدث عند تناول هذه الأجسام دليل على أنه لا يجوز أن يكون الموجِبُ (٤) لشيء منه بعض الأجسام الذي هو مجانس لسائرها، وأنّ(ه) الشبع والري والإسكار لو وجبت عن تناول الطعام والشراب لوجب حدوث ذلك عند تناول الحصى (٦) والتراب والفث (٧) والحنظل وأن يحدث الري عند شرب الخل والبلسان (۸) وسائر المائعات والجامدات أيضاً لأنها والإسكار من جنس الطعام والشراب .
And among what also indicates the corruption of what they hold to, from affirming the action of natures, is that if intoxication, burning, cooling, heating, satiety, quenching of thirst, and other (1) occurring matters were occurring (2) from a nature among natures, then that nature would not be free from (3) being the very body itself that is impressed, or a meaning other than it. For if it is the body itself, then the consumption of all bodies would necessitate the occurrence of intoxication, satiety, and quenching of thirst, and the proximity of every body would necessitate cooling and heating, because it has been established by proof that all bodies are of one genus, and it is known that if a thing necessitates a matter and produces an effect, then what is like it and homogeneous to it must necessitate a similar ruling and effect, like the two blacknesses existing in a substrate, and the two motions in a single direction, and what runs their course among genera. And in the knowledge of the difference in what occurs upon consumption of these bodies is evidence that the necessitator (4) of any of it cannot be some bodies which are homogeneous to the rest, and that satiety, quenching of thirst, and intoxication, if they were necessitated by the consumption of food and drink, it would be necessary for that to occur upon the consumption of pebbles (6), dirt, futh (7), and colocynth, and that quenching of thirst would occur upon drinking vinegar, balsam (8), and all liquids and solids as well, because they and intoxication are of the genus of food and drink.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is an argument (dalil) against the theory that natural phenomena are caused by inherent natures (taba'i') in bodies. The author begins by stating that if effects like burning, cooling, satiety, etc., were produced by a nature (tab'), then that nature must either be identical to the body itself or an additional attribute ('arad). In this first argument, he posits the case where the nature is the body itself. He argues that if the body as a whole were the cause, then all bodies of the same genus—being homogeneous—would produce identical effects. For instance, if fire's heat came from its fire-nature, all bodies would produce heat when touched. Since we observe that different bodies cause different effects (e.g., food satiates, stones do not), this contradicts the claim. The author uses the principle that homogeneous things produce homogeneous effects, like two blacknesses in the same location. Therefore, the variability in effects proves that the cause cannot be the body itself.

Key Terms
طبيعة (tabīʿa)
جسم (jism)
جنس (jins)
موجب (mūjib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Baqillani is denying all natural causation.
Correction: He is only rejecting the idea that natures are inherent in bodies as identical to them, not necessarily denying that bodies have any causal properties at all.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, if the nature were identical to the body, what would necessarily follow?
A) All bodies of the same genus would produce the same effects.
B) Bodies would have no effects at all.
C) Only some bodies would produce effects.
D) Effects would be random.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the identity of nature with body by arguing that if it were so, all bodies of the same genus would produce identical effects, contradicting observed diversity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see that bread satiates but stones do not, do you truly attribute that difference to Allah's direct creation, or do you unconsciously rely on an inherent 'nature' in bread? How would your gratitude change if you believed that Allah creates satiety at every meal without any necessary cause in the bread itself?
?
Baqillani argues that if natures were identical to bodies, all bodies would produce identical effects. Since they don't, the cause must be something else. Does your daily life reflect a deep conviction that Allah is the immediate cause of every effect, or do you functionally live as if things have independent powers? What would you need to change to make your belief consistent?

Accidents Cannot Be Agents
First argument: accidents are not agents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's first argument that accidents cannot be agents because they lack the attributes necessary for action.

Core Idea
Accidents are too weak to cause any effect—only a living agent can act.

Baqillani begins his refutation by attacking the very idea that an accident could be an agent. He points out that accidents like colors and spatial modes cannot perform actions, and neither can inanimate objects. This sets the stage for a powerful argument that natures, if they are accidents, cannot produce the effects attributed to them.

Guided Path
1
Read the first argument
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key claim: accidents cannot be agents (fāʿila).
2
Identify the examples
Baqillani gives examples of accidents (colors, spatial modes) and inanimate things to show they cannot produce intricate effects like craftsmanship.
Think about why these cannot act.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that if natures are accidents, they cannot be agents, so they cannot cause the effects attributed to them.

Source Text

وإن كان ذلك الطبع الذي يومئون إليه عرضاً من الأعراض فسدّ إثباته من وجوه : أحدها أن الأعراض لا يجوز أن تكون فاعلة كما لا يجوز أن تفعل الأفعال الألوان والأكوانُ وغَيْرُهُما من أجناس الأعراض، وكما لا يجوز أن يَصْنَع دقائق المحكمات من الصياغة والنساجة والكتابة شيء من الأعراض ولا الميت ولا الجماد . وعلى أنه لو جاز وقوع هذه الأفعال من الشبع والري والإسكار والصحة والإسقام من الأعراض لجاز وقوعها من الموات ولو جاز ذلك لجاز أن نفعل نحن جميع ذلك لأننا قادرون عالمون مريدون، فوقوع هذه الأفعال من الحي العالم القادر أقربُ في عقل كل عاقل من وقوعها من الأعراض والموات وفي تعذر ذلك علينا دليل على أنه أشد تعذراً على من قَصُرَ عن صفتنا . ولأن هذه الأمور لو كانت حادثة عن طبائع هي أعراض موجودة بهذه الأجسام المطبوعة نحو النار والطعام والشراب لم تخل تلك الأعراض من أن تكون موجودة بالأجسام عن طبيعة أو غير طبيعة فإن كانت موجودة بها عن طبيعة أخرى وجب تعلق ذلك بما لا غاية له كما بيناه من قبل وإن كانت موجودةً عن غير طبيعة جاز أن تحتمل أيضاً الأجسام وجود الإسكار والشبع والريّ عن غير طبيعة توجب ذلك وهذا يبطل إثبات الطباع (۱) إبطالاً ظاهراً .
And if that nature which they indicate is an accident among accidents, then its affirmation is corrupt from aspects: one of them is that accidents are not permissible as agents, just as it is not permissible for actions to be done by colors, spatial modes, and other genera of accidents, and just as it is not permissible for any accident, nor a dead thing, nor an inanimate thing to produce the intricacies of masterworks of craftsmanship, weaving, and writing. And also, if the occurrence of these actions—satiety, quenching of thirst, intoxication, health, and illness—from accidents were permissible, then their occurrence from inanimate things would be permissible; and if that were permissible, it would be permissible for us ourselves to do all of that because we are powerful, knowing, willing. So the occurrence of these actions from a living, knowing, powerful being is more reasonable in the mind of every rational person than their occurrence from accidents and inanimate things, and in the impossibility of that for us is proof that it is even more impossible for that which falls short of our attribute. And because if these matters were occurring from natures that are accidents existing in these impressed bodies, like fire, food, and drink, those accidents would not be free from being existent in the bodies from a nature or not from a nature. If they are existent in them from another nature, then an infinite regress would be necessary, as we have explained before; and if they are existent not from a nature, then it would be possible for bodies also to bear the existence of intoxication, satiety, and quenching of thirst from no nature that necessitates that, and this nullifies the affirmation of natures (1) with clear nullification.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents multiple arguments (wujuh) against the thesis that natures are accidents. The author lists them: Firstly, accidents cannot be agents (fa'ila) because they are not living, knowing, or powerful; they cannot produce intricate effects like craftsmanship or writing. If accidents could cause effects like satiety, then even inanimate objects could cause them, which leads to absurdity. Since we humans, who are living and powerful, cannot produce such effects directly, it is even more unlikely that accidents can. Secondly, if natures are accidents existing in bodies, those accidents themselves must either be caused by another nature (leading to infinite regress) or not. If not caused by a nature, then the original effects could also occur without a nature, undermining the whole theory. This argument is a reductio ad absurdum.

فاعل
موات

Misconception: One might think that accidents can cause effects if they are 'natures' inherent in bodies.
Correction: Baqillani shows that accidents are inherently passive; they cannot be agents regardless of being called natures.

Checkpoint
What is one reason Baqillani gives for why accidents cannot be agents?
A) Accidents are eternal.
B) Accidents are not alive, knowing, or powerful.
C) Accidents can only cause similar accidents.
D) Accidents are not material.

Key Takeaway
Accidents, lacking life, knowledge, and power, cannot be agents, so natures cannot be accidents if they are to cause effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You trust your phone to wake you up, your car to get you to work, and your own body to carry you through the day. But if all these are made of accidents with no power, who is truly sustaining you? Take a moment to thank the only true Agent—Allah—and ask Him to keep you safe in every moment.
?
When you feel weak and unable to change your situation, do you remember that even your own efforts are just accidents created by Allah? Then why do you despair? Repent for relying on your own strength, and commit to asking Allah alone to act for you.
?
Baqillani points out that a dead body cannot weave a cloth or write a book. Yet you often attribute your accomplishments to your own skill and hard work. Write down one achievement you are proud of and consciously attribute it entirely to Allah's power, not your own.

The Absurdity of Accidental Causality
Second argument: reductio ad absurdum of accidental causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's reductio ad absurdum argument against accidental causation of phenomena like satiety and intoxication.

Core Idea
If accidents cause effects, then dead matter could too—an absurd conclusion.

Baqillani extends his argument by considering what would follow if accidents could cause specific effects like satiety. He shows that this would imply that even inanimate objects could cause these effects, which is clearly false. Moreover, since we humans cannot cause these effects directly, it is even more absurd to attribute them to accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read the passage carefully. Notice the conditional structure: if accidents cause effects, then inanimate things could too.
2
Analyze the absurd consequences
If satiety, intoxication, etc., could come from accidents, then dead matter could also produce them, which is absurd.
Think about why dead matter cannot produce these.
3
Compare with human inability
We humans, who are living and powerful, cannot cause these effects directly; so it is even more impossible for accidents.
This strengthens the argument.

The Infinite Regress of Natures
Third argument: dilemma of infinite regress or self-nullification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's third argument that assuming natures are accidents leads to an infinite regress or a self-defeating alternative.

Core Idea
If natures are accidents, they either depend on another nature (infinite regress) or not (undermining the theory).

In this final argument, Baqillani examines the logical implications of the claim that natures are accidents existing in bodies. He asks whether those accidents themselves exist from a nature or not. If from a nature, infinite regress; if not, then the original effects could also occur without a nature, eliminating the need for natures altogether.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument
Read the passage carefully. Focus on the two possibilities: the accidents exist from a nature or not.
2
Analyze the first horn: infinite regress
If the accidents exist from another nature, then that nature would also require another, leading to an infinite chain.
Baqillani references a previous explanation.
3
Analyze the second horn: self-undermining
If the accidents exist without a nature, then the bodies themselves could exhibit the effects without a nature, negating the need for natures.
4
Conclusion
Both options are impossible or undermine the theory, so the affirmation of natures as accidents is nullified.

Baqillani's Final Refutation of Natures
Baqillani demands specification of natures and argues from parity of events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's two final arguments against the theory of natures: the demand to specify what natures are and the parity argument from the dependence of all originated events.

Core Idea
If blind natures cause pain, they could also cause thought—impossible; all events need God.

Baqillani concludes his refutation of natures with two powerful blows. First, he challenges his opponents to specify what kind of accidents these natures are—a demand they cannot meet. Then he uses a parity argument: if low-level events like pain could occur from a lifeless nature, then high-level acts like will and writing could also be attributed to such a nature, which is absurd. The passage establishes that all originated events, without exception, require a living, powerful agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the two distinct arguments: the opening challenge and the longer parity argument that follows.
The numbers in parentheses are editorial markers; ignore them.
2
Identify the two arguments
The first argument is a demand: 'Inform us about these natures—from which genus of accidents are they?' The second is a conditional chain: if some events need no agent, then all events would need no agent, which is false.
Notice that the second argument uses a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the logical structure
The key move is that all events share the property of being originated (hadith). Therefore, if any event could happen without a creator, all could. Since we know will and reflection require a living, knowing agent, all events require such an agent.
This establishes God as the sole creator.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Baqillani concludes that every originated event needs a 'living, powerful agent'—this is a direct proof for the existence of God as the creator of all things.
This refutes the theory of natures which claimed some events arise from blind natural forces.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم أيضاً خَبِّرونا عن هذه الطبائع من أي أجناس الأعراض : هي؟ ومن أي قبيل هي ؟ فلا يجدون إلى ذكر شيءٍ سبيلاً . ومما يدل (۲) على فساد فعل الطباع أيضاً أنه (۳) لو جاز وقوع بعض الحوادث من الشبع والري والصحة والسقم واللذة والألم من طبع ليس بحيّ ولا قادر ولا قاصد لجاز وقوع الإرادة والنظر والكتابة ودقائق الصياغة والنجارة من طبع وعن طبع ليس بحي ولا قادر ولا عالم كما أنه لو جاز استغناء بعض الحوادث عن مُحْدِث لجاز غنى (٤) سائرها عن ذلك، فلما كان جهة تعلّق هي كونُها فِعلاً حادثاً دون كونها إرادة، ثبت أن سائر الحوادث المشاركة للإرادة في وصف (٥) الحدوث محتاجة إلى ما تحتاج إليه الإرادة من فاعل حيّ قادر .
And it is also said to them: Inform us about these natures: from which genus of accidents are they? And of what kind are they? But they find no way to mention anything. And among what also indicates (2) the corruption of the action of natures is that (3) if the occurrence of some events—satiety, quenching of thirst, health, sickness, pleasure, and pain—from a nature that is not living, powerful, nor purposive were permissible, then the occurrence of will, reflection, writing, and intricacies of craftsmanship and carpentry from a nature that is not living, powerful, nor knowing would be permissible; just as if the independence of some events from an originator were permissible, then the independence of all of them from that would be permissible. So since the aspect of their commonality is that they are a happening act, not that they are will, it is established that all events which share with will the attribute (5) of being originated are in need of what the will is in need of: a living, powerful agent.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage concludes the refutation with two final arguments. First, a challenge: the opponents cannot specify what kind of accidents these natures are, revealing their lack of evidence. Second, an argument from parity: if lower events (like satiety, pain) could be caused by a non-living, non-powerful nature, then higher events (like will, thought, writing) could also be caused by such a nature—which is absurd because we know these require a living, knowing agent. Since all events share the property of being originated (hadith), they must all require the same kind of agent: a living, powerful being (i.e., God). The argument uses a reductio ad absurdum and establishes that all events, including those attributed to natures, necessitate a Creator.

Key Terms
حادث (ḥādith)
فاعل حيّ قادر (fāʿil ḥayy qādir)
إرادة (irāda)
نظر (naẓar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Baqillani denies any secondary causality and claims events are random.
Correction: Baqillani does not deny that events occur, but he insists that the ultimate cause of all originated events is a living, powerful agent (God), not blind natures.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani conclude from the fact that both will events and physical events share the property of being originated?
A) They require different types of agents.
B) Both require a living, powerful agent.
C) Physical events can occur without an agent.
D) Only will events need an agent.

Key Takeaway
All originated events, whether physical or mental, require a living, powerful agent—God alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever attribute your successes to your own 'nature'—like talent, hard work, or luck—rather than to God? Baqillani's argument shows that even intricate acts require a living, powerful agent. Will you consciously thank God for every originated act you perform today?
?
Is there any area of your life you still believe you can manage without divine help? The parity argument says if one event could be independent, all could. Would you be willing to entrust every detail of your day—including your thoughts and decisions—entirely to God?
?
Baqillani asks the proponents to define the genus of natures, and they cannot. Have you ever held a belief about how things work (e.g., 'this medicine heals') without truly understanding its cause? How can you reorient your certainty from natural causes to the Creator alone?
