5 min
The Reductio Against Accidents as Causes
Baqillani argues that if accidents cause effects, they would do so in inanimate objects too.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that accidents cannot be genuine causes of effects, and that only a living, powerful agent can produce such effects.

Core Idea
Accidents can't cause effects; even we can't cause satiety at will.

Imam Baqillani presents a sharp logical argument to refute the claim that accidents (like heat or moisture) can be causes of effects such as satiety or intoxication. He uses a reductio ad absurdum, showing that if accidents could cause these, then even inanimate objects would cause them, and we as powerful beings would certainly be able to produce them. Since we cannot, the original claim is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully. It is a single continuous argument. Note the key terms 'أعراض' (accidents) and 'موات' (inanimate objects).
The passage uses a conditional structure: 'لو جاز... لجاز...' (if it were permissible... then it would be permissible).
2
Understand the hypothetical
Baqillani starts with the assumption that accidents can cause effects like satiety. Follow the logic: if true, then inanimate objects (which have accidents) could also cause them.
This is the first step of the reductio.
3
Apply to human agents
If inanimate objects could cause such effects, then certainly humans, who are powerful, knowing, and willing, should be able to cause them even more easily. But we cannot voluntarily produce satiety in ourselves.
This step highlights the absurdity.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since we cannot cause satiety at will, it is even more impossible for accidents or inanimate objects to do so. Therefore, such effects require a living, powerful agent beyond human capacity—God.
This is the core theological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنه لو جاز وقوع هذه الأفعال من الشبع والري والإسكار والصحة والإسقام من الأعراض لجاز وقوعها من الموات ولو جاز ذلك لجاز أن نفعل نحن جميع ذلك لأننا قادرون عالمون مريدون، فوقوع هذه الأفعال من الحي العالم القادر أقربُ في عقل كـل عـاقـل من وقوعهـا من الأعراض والموات وفي تعذر ذلك علينا دليل على أنه أشد تعذراً على من قَصُرَ عن صفتنا .
And that if it were permissible for these actions of satiety, thirst-quenching, intoxication, health, and sickness to occur from accidents, it would be permissible for them to occur from inanimate objects. And if that were permissible, then we ourselves could do all of that because we are powerful, knowing, willing. So the occurrence of these actions from a living, knowing, powerful being is more plausible in the mind of every rational person than their occurrence from accidents and inanimate objects. And in the impossibility of that for us is proof that it is even more impossible for one who falls short of our attribute.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy and reductio ad absurdum. Baqillani argues that if accidents (like temperature or moisture) could produce effects like satiety, intoxication, etc., then even inanimate objects (which have these accidents) could produce them. But even more, we as living, knowing, powerful beings would be able to produce them at will. Since we cannot (we cannot make ourselves full by just thinking), it is even more impossible for accidents or inanimate objects to do so. Thus, such effects require a living, powerful agent, i.e., God.

Key Terms
أعراض
موات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that natural causes (accidents) can produce effects without a living agent.
Correction: Baqillani shows that if accidents could cause effects, then inanimate objects would also cause them, and we as living beings would be even more capable. Since we cannot, accidents are not true causes.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani's argument, why is it impossible for accidents to produce effects like satiety?
A) Because accidents don't exist
B) Because we are more powerful than accidents and cannot produce them
C) Because satiety only comes from food
D) Because accidents are not living

Key Takeaway
Baqillani uses a reductio ad absurdum to prove that accidents cannot be causes; only a living, powerful agent like God can produce such effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel full after a meal, do you ever stop to consider that you did not cause that satiety? That it is a direct gift from the Living, Knowing, Powerful Creator? How does that realization change how you view your own body and its functions?
?
Baqillani argues that if accidents could cause effects, we could do it ourselves. Think of a time you desperately wanted to change something in your body (e.g., fall asleep, feel less pain) but couldn't. What does that teach you about who truly controls these things? Will you acknowledge His power every time your body functions?
?
The passage shows that even with all our power and knowledge, we cannot produce satiety at will. Does your daily attitude reflect a reliance on God for the most basic functions, or do you take them for granted? What will you change today?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

Baqillani's Two Refutations of the Theory of Natures
Baqillani presents two arguments against natures as causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's two arguments against the theory that events are caused by natures: a dilemma leading to infinite regress or incoherence, and a challenge to define what kind of accidents natures are.

Core Idea
Natures cannot explain causation without causing infinite regress or being arbitrary.

Baqillani presents two powerful refutations of the claim that events are caused by 'natures' (ṭabā'iʿ) inherent in bodies. The first argument shows the theory leads to an infinite regress or becomes self-defeating. The second argues that proponents cannot even define what these natures are, revealing the concept as incoherent. This lesson examines these arguments step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let Baqillani's argument structure become clear.
The passage contains two distinct arguments; note where the second begins.
2
Analyze the first argument (dilemma)
Identify the dilemma: either natures themselves require another nature (leading to infinite regress) or they do not (making the theory arbitrary). Understand why both options are problematic for the claim that events are caused by natures.
Baqillani references an earlier proof that infinite regress is impossible.
3
Analyze the second argument (definition challenge)
Notice the challenge: proponents cannot specify from which genus or category of accidents these natures come. This shows the concept is not clearly defined and thus cannot be a valid explanation.
This is a direct request for concrete definition.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these arguments are effective in Ashʿarī theology. They demonstrate that alternative causal theories fail, supporting the Ashʿarī view that God is the direct cause of events.
This connects to broader kalām debates on causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأن هذه الأمور لو كانت حادثة عن طبائع هي أعراض موجودة بهذه الأجسام المطبوعة نحو النار والطعام والشراب لم تخل تلك الأعراض من تكون موجودة بالأجسام عن طبيعة أو غير طبيعة فإن كانت موجودة بها عن طبيعة أخرى وجب تعلق ذلك بما لا غاية له كما بيناه من قبل، وإن كانت موجودةً عن غير طبيعة جاز أن تحتمل أيضاً الأجسام وجود الإسكار والشبع والريّ عن غير طبيعة توجب ذلك، وهذا يبطل إثبات الطباع إبطالاً ظاهراً . ويقال لهم أيضاً خَبِّرونا عن هذه الطبائع من أي أجناس الأعراض هي؟ ومن أي قبيل هي ؟ فلا يجدون إلى ذكر شيءٍ سبيلاً .
And because if these things were contingent upon natures that are accidents existing in these imprinted bodies like fire, food, and drink, those accidents would not be free from either being present in bodies by a nature or not by a nature. If they are present by another nature, then it would necessitate an infinite regress as we have explained before. And if they are present not by a nature, then it would be possible for bodies to sustain the occurrence of intoxication, satiety, and thirst-quenching without a nature that necessitates that. And this clearly invalidates the affirmation of natures. And it is also said to them: Inform us about these natures—from which genus of accidents are they? And from which category? So they will not find any way to mention anything.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage contains two refutations of the theory that natures (tabā'i') cause events. The first is a dilemma: either the accidents that are supposed to be natures exist in bodies by their own nature or not. If they exist by another nature, that leads to infinite regress, since each nature would require another nature to account for its existence. If they exist not by a nature, then they are not necessary, and bodies could have the effects (like intoxication, satiety) without the supposed natures. The second refutation is a challenge to the proponents: they cannot specify what kind of accidents these natures are, showing the concept is incoherent.

Key Terms
طبائع
أعراض
أجسام مطبوعة
لا غاية له

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natures are self-explanatory and do not need further explanation.
Correction: Baqillani shows that if natures exist, they themselves require a cause (either another nature or not), leading to either infinite regress or arbitrariness.

Checkpoint
What is one problem Baqillani points out with the theory of natures?
A) Natures are eternal
B) If natures cause by themselves, they either rely on other natures or not, both problematic
C) Natures are not real
D) Natures can only be in living things

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the theory of natures by showing it leads to either infinite regress or incoherence, and by challenging its proponents to define the concept clearly—a task they cannot fulfill.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a good or bad event, do you instinctively attribute it to a 'natural cause' or do you remember that it is God's direct act? What does your immediate reaction reveal about your heart's true reliance?
?
Baqillani challenges the proponents to define the nature of things. Can you define the 'nature' of your own habits or character? What does your inability to do so teach you about depending on anything other than Allah?
?
If natures are not real causes, then your life's events are directly from Allah. Are you living as though each moment is a direct gift from Him, or do you take things for granted as 'just how the world works'?

Causality and Created Things

All Contingent Events Require a Living Agent
Baqillani refutes the theory of natures by parity of reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that the need for a living, powerful agent is based on an event's contingency, not its specific type.

Core Idea
Contingency, not type, determines the need for a living cause.

Baqillani presents a powerful parity argument against the idea that some events can arise from unconscious natures. He shows that if we allow that for some events, we cannot logically deny it for all, including will and craftsmanship. The argument establishes that the basis for requiring a living agent is the event's contingency, not its specific nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: a conditional statement begins the argument.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لو جاز' (if it were permissible) which sets up the reductio.
2
Identify the conditional argument
Baqillani argues that if some events (like satiety and pain) could arise from a non-living nature, then logically all events (including will and craftsmanship) could also arise from such a nature. This is the core of the parity reasoning.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since the consequence is absurd (we know will and craftsmanship require a living agent), the original permission is false. Baqillani then states that the reason all events need a living agent is their common property of being contingent (حادث).
The phrase 'جهة تعلّق هي كونها فعلاً حادثاً' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل على فساد فعل الطباع أيضاً أنه لو جاز وقوع بعض الحوادث من الشبع والري والصحة والسقم واللذة والألم من طبع ليس بحيّ ولا قادر ولا قاصد لجاز وقوع الإرادة والنظر والكتابة ودقائق الصياغة والنجارة من طبع وعن طبع ليس بحي ولا قادر ولا عالم كما أنه لو جاز استغناء بعض الحوادث عن مُحْدِث لجاز غنى سائرها عن ذلك، فلما كان جهة تعلّق هي كونُها فِعلاً حادثاً دون كونها إرادة، ثبت أن سائر الحوادث المشاركة للإرادة في وصف الحدوث محتاجة إلى ما تحتاج إليه الإرادة من فاعل حيّ قادر .
And among what also indicates the corruption of the action of natures is that if it were permissible for some events to occur from satiety, thirst-quenching, health, sickness, pleasure, and pain from a nature that is not living, powerful, or intending, then it would be permissible for will, reflection, writing, detailed craftsmanship, and carpentry to occur from a nature that is not living, powerful, or knowing, just as if it were permissible for some events to dispense with an originator, it would be permissible for all others to dispense with that. Since the aspect of dependence is that they are contingent acts, not that they are will, it is established that all events that share with will the description of contingency are in need of what will is in need of: a living, powerful agent.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This is a parity argument. Baqillani argues that if you allow that some events (like physical effects) can come from a non-living, non-powerful nature, then you cannot deny that more complex events (like will, craftsmanship) could also come from such a nature, which is absurd. The key is that the need for a living, powerful agent is due to the event's contingency (being a new event), not its specific type. Since all events are contingent, they all require a living, powerful agent.

Key Terms
حادث
طبع
محدث
إرادة
حي
قادر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the basis for all events needing a living, powerful agent?
A) Their specific type
B) Their contingency
C) Their complexity
D) Their necessity

Key Takeaway
All contingent events, whether physical sensations or intellectual acts, require a living, powerful agent because of their shared property of being originated (حدث).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a simple pleasure like satiety after a meal, do you instinctively attribute it to the food itself, or do you consciously recognize that the Living, Powerful Agent directly originated that feeling? What one habit could you adopt this week to remind yourself of His direct involvement in every sensation?
?
Baqillani argues that if even one event could occur without a creator, then all could. Is there any area of your life where you subconsciously act as if 'it just happens' (like your health, your mood, your daily provisions)? How would your reliance on Allah change if you truly believed that not a single contingent moment exists without His creative act?
?
The argument shows that your own will and thoughts are also contingent events, created by Allah each moment. Does that truth make you feel less responsible for your choices, or does it deepen your sense of accountability knowing that your will is a gift from Him? Commit to one practical way you will use your will today to seek His pleasure.

The Principle of Consistent Causation and Its Implications
Baqillani states the principle of consistent causation and applies it to examples of knowledge, will, and striking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that every effect has a consistent cause, and how this principle undermines the theory of natures when applied to examples like knowledge, will, and striking.

Core Idea
If natures were real, their effects would be constant; they are not.

Baqillani begins by stating a foundational principle: every effect has a cause, and when the cause repeats, the effect repeats. He then applies this to the theory of natures, showing that natural causes like striking should produce consistent results, but they don't always, pointing to a willful agent behind events.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on consistent causation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, focusing on the principle that every effect must have a cause and that causes produce consistent effects when repeated.
The passage repeats the idea of multiplicity of causes leading to multiplicity of effects.
2
Analyze the knower and willer example
Notice the conditional: if a knower knows something, he knows it whenever he has knowledge; similarly for will. This shows consistency.
This example illustrates the principle in human actions.
3
Apply the principle to striking
According to those who believe in generation of pain from striking, more strikes should cause more pain. Ask yourself: does this always happen?
The passage states that both pain and the body's disappearance increase with more strikes.
4
Identify the problem for natures
If natures were real and inherent, they would produce constant effects under constant conditions. But empirical observation shows otherwise, thus refuting the theory.
The conclusion is that if they were from natures, they would be necessary, but they are not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل أيضاً على إبطال قولهم بفعل الطبائع علمنا بوجوب وجود كل معلول بعلة، كلما وُجدت وتكررت وكلما وُجد مثلها، ووجوب كثرة المُسَبَّبات عند كثرة أسبابها على قول من أثبت السبب والمسبب. ألا ترى إلى وجوب كون العالم عالماً بالشيء والمريد مريداً له كلما تكررت له الإرادة والعلم وُجِدَ به أمثالها في كل وقت وزمان ولم يَجُز أن توجد بـه الحكم في بعض الأماكن والأزمان ولا يوجد الحكم؟ وكذلك يجب عند القائلين بتولد الألم عن الضرب وذهـاب الجسم عند الدفعة أن يكثرا عند كثرة أسبابهما ويشتدّا عند كثرة الضرب والاعتماد عليه والدفع . وكذلك يجب لو كان الإسكارُ والشبع والري ونماء الزرع حادثاً عن طبع الشراب والطعام والسقي والتسميد وحمى الشمس لوجب أن تزداد هذه الأمور ما كانت الأجسام محتملة لها عند وجود أمثال ما أوجب ذلك وتناوله فكان يجب أن يزيد الزرع ويَنْمِي وإن بلغ حد النهاية في مستقر العادة إذا أديمَ سَقيه وأكثر تسميده وإظهاره للشمس حتى يزيد أبدا وينمي وأن توجب له هذه الأمور الزيادة في غير إبان الزرع وحينه كما توجب ذلك في وقت عادة خروجه وفي علمنا أن السقي والتسميد يعود بتلفه إذا بلغ مقداراً ما وأنه لا يوجب له في ذلك غير حين نمائه دليل على سقوط ما قالوه، وكذلك فلو أن الإنسان أكل وشرب فوق شبعه لم يحدث له أبداً من ذلك الشبع والرِيّ ما يحدث عند الحاجة إلى تناول الطعام والشراب بل يصير ضرراً وألماً وإذا كان هذا هكذا وجب بطلان ما قالوه، وفسد أن تكون الطبائع.
And among what also indicates the invalidation of their statement about the action of natures is our knowledge of the necessity of every effect having a cause, whenever it exists and is repeated, and whenever its like exists, and the necessity of the multiplicity of effects when their causes multiply, according to the one who affirms cause and effect. Do you not see the necessity of the knower knowing something and the willer willing it whenever his will and knowledge are repeated, and their likes exist in every time and place, and it is not permissible that the judgment exists in some places and times and not exist? And likewise, according to those who claim the generation of pain from striking and the disappearance of the body from the push, both must increase when their causes increase and intensify with many strikes, reliance, and pushing. And likewise, if intoxication, satiety, thirst-quenching, and growth of the crop were contingent upon the nature of drink, food, watering, fertilizing, and the heat of the sun, then these things would necessarily increase as long as bodies could sustain them when similar causes exist and are applied. So it would be necessary for the crop to increase and grow even if it reached the ultimate limit in the customary course, if watering is continued and fertilizing is increased and exposure to the sun is extended, so that it would increase forever and grow, and these things would necessitate increase outside of its season and time, just as they necessitate it at the time of its customary emergence. And in our knowledge that watering and fertilizing result in its destruction when it reaches a certain amount, and that they do not necessitate that for it except at the time of its growth, is evidence of the failure of what they say. Likewise, if a person eats and drinks beyond his fill, there would never occur from that the satiety and thirst-quenching that occurs when in need of food and drink, but rather it becomes harm and pain. And when this is so, their statement is necessarily invalid, and the existence of natures is corrupted.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is an empirical argument against the theory of natures. Baqillani first states the principle that every effect has a cause, and when the cause is repeated, the effect is repeated; when causes multiply, effects multiply. He gives as example that a knower knows whenever he has knowledge, in all times and places. Also, those who believe in generation of pain from hitting say that pain increases with more hits. Then he applies this to natures: if the nature of food caused satiety, then eating more should cause more satiety, but beyond a certain point it causes pain, not satiety. If the nature of watering caused plant growth, then more watering would make it grow indefinitely, but in fact overwatering kills it. This shows that the supposed natures are not consistent causes; their effects are not necessary but depend on other conditions, indicating that events are not produced by fixed natures but by a willful agent.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
سبب
مسبب
تولد
طبع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natures are consistent causes that always produce the same effects.
Correction: Baqillani shows that if natures were consistent, effects would be constant under the same conditions, but they are not, indicating that events are not produced by fixed natures.

Checkpoint
What principle does Baqillani use to evaluate the theory of natures?
A) Effects are independent of causes
B) Every effect has a cause, and the same cause produces the same effect
C) Natures are unknowable
D) Only human actions have causes

Key Takeaway
Consistent causation implies that if natures were real, their effects would be constant, but they are not, thus invalidating the theory of natures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a repeated pattern in your life—like success after hard work or pain after a mistake—do you instinctively see it as a natural law or as a direct sign from Allah? What would change in your heart if you truly saw every effect as directly from Him?
?
Baqillani argues that if natures were real, their effects would be constant. Think of a time when you expected a consistent outcome but it didn't happen. How did that moment shake your assumptions? Did you turn to Allah or just look for another natural explanation?
?
Is there an area of your life where you rely on 'the way things work' instead of trusting Allah's direct will? What would it take for you to see that every breath, every pain, and every joy is a direct creation of His, not a product of blind nature? Pray for that seeing.

Empirical Refutation of Natures through Plant Growth and Overeating
Baqillani uses plant growth and eating examples to show the inconsistency of natural causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the empirical examples of plant growth and overeating demonstrate the inconsistency of natural causes, thereby refuting the theory of natures.

Core Idea
Overwatering kills plants and overeating causes pain, showing no fixed nature.

Baqillani now turns to two concrete examples: plant growth and eating. If the nature of water caused growth, then more water would always cause more growth, but in reality overwatering kills. Likewise, if the nature of food caused satiety, eating more would always cause more satiety, but beyond a point it causes pain. This inconsistency refutes the theory of natures.

Guided Path
1
Read the plant growth example
Read the hypothetical: if the nature of watering and fertilizing caused growth, then continuous watering should produce continuous growth, even beyond the usual season and limit.
The passage says: 'it would be necessary for the crop to increase and grow even if it reached the ultimate limit.'
2
Contrast with reality
In reality, overwatering destroys the plant. Baqillani says: 'watering and fertilizing result in its destruction when it reaches a certain amount.' This shows the effect is not from a fixed nature.
The destruction at a certain point indicates a willful agent that sets limits.
3
Read the eating example
Similarly, if the nature of food caused satiety, then eating more should always produce more satiety. But beyond the need, it causes harm and pain.
The passage states: 'if a person eats and drinks beyond his fill, there would never occur from that the satiety... but rather it becomes harm and pain.'
4
Conclude the invalidation
These inconsistencies show that events are not caused by inherent natures but by a wise Agent who creates effects according to His will and wisdom.
Baqillani ends: 'their statement is necessarily invalid, and the existence of natures is corrupted.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل أيضاً على إبطال قولهم بفعل الطبائع علمنا بوجوب وجود كل معلول بعلة، كلما وُجدت وتكررت وكلما وُجد مثلها، ووجوب كثرة المُسَبَّبات عند كثرة أسبابها على قول من أثبت السبب والمسبب. ألا ترى إلى وجوب كون العالم عالماً بالشيء والمريد مريداً له كلما تكررت له الإرادة والعلم وُجِدَ به أمثالها في كل وقت وزمان ولم يَجُز أن توجد بـه الحكم في بعض الأماكن والأزمان ولا يوجد الحكم؟ وكذلك يجب عند القائلين بتولد الألم عن الضرب وذهـاب الجسم عند الدفعة أن يكثرا عند كثرة أسبابهما ويشتدّا عند كثرة الضرب والاعتماد عليه والدفع . وكذلك يجب لو كان الإسكارُ والشبع والري ونماء الزرع حادثاً عن طبع الشراب والطعام والسقي والتسميد وحمى الشمس لوجب أن تزداد هذه الأمور ما كانت الأجسام محتملة لها عند وجود أمثال ما أوجب ذلك وتناوله فكان يجب أن يزيد الزرع ويَنْمِي وإن بلغ حد النهاية في مستقر العادة إذا أديمَ سَقيه وأكثر تسميده وإظهاره للشمس حتى يزيد أبدا وينمي وأن توجب له هذه الأمور الزيادة في غير إبان الزرع وحينه كما توجب ذلك في وقت عادة خروجه وفي علمنا أن السقي والتسميد يعود بتلفه إذا بلغ مقداراً ما وأنه لا يوجب له في ذلك غير حين نمائه دليل على سقوط ما قالوه، وكذلك فلو أن الإنسان أكل وشرب فوق شبعه لم يحدث له أبداً من ذلك الشبع والرِيّ ما يحدث عند الحاجة إلى تناول الطعام والشراب بل يصير ضرراً وألماً وإذا كان هذا هكذا وجب بطلان ما قالوه، وفسد أن تكون الطبائع.
And among what also indicates the invalidation of their statement about the action of natures is our knowledge of the necessity of every effect having a cause, whenever it exists and is repeated, and whenever its like exists, and the necessity of the multiplicity of effects when their causes multiply, according to the one who affirms cause and effect. Do you not see the necessity of the knower knowing something and the willer willing it whenever his will and knowledge are repeated, and their likes exist in every time and place, and it is not permissible that the judgment exists in some places and times and not exist? And likewise, according to those who claim the generation of pain from striking and the disappearance of the body from the push, both must increase when their causes increase and intensify with many strikes, reliance, and pushing. And likewise, if intoxication, satiety, thirst-quenching, and growth of the crop were contingent upon the nature of drink, food, watering, fertilizing, and the heat of the sun, then these things would necessarily increase as long as bodies could sustain them when similar causes exist and are applied. So it would be necessary for the crop to increase and grow even if it reached the ultimate limit in the customary course, if watering is continued and fertilizing is increased and exposure to the sun is extended, so that it would increase forever and grow, and these things would necessitate increase outside of its season and time, just as they necessitate it at the time of its customary emergence. And in our knowledge that watering and fertilizing result in its destruction when it reaches a certain amount, and that they do not necessitate that for it except at the time of its growth, is evidence of the failure of what they say. Likewise, if a person eats and drinks beyond his fill, there would never occur from that the satiety and thirst-quenching that occurs when in need of food and drink, but rather it becomes harm and pain. And when this is so, their statement is necessarily invalid, and the existence of natures is corrupted.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is an empirical argument against the theory of natures. Baqillani first states the principle that every effect has a cause, and when the cause is repeated, the effect is repeated; when causes multiply, effects multiply. He gives as example that a knower knows whenever he has knowledge, in all times and places. Also, those who believe in generation of pain from hitting say that pain increases with more hits. Then he applies this to natures: if the nature of food caused satiety, then eating more should cause more satiety, but beyond a certain point it causes pain, not satiety. If the nature of watering caused plant growth, then more watering would make it grow indefinitely, but in fact overwatering kills it. This shows that the supposed natures are not consistent causes; their effects are not necessary but depend on other conditions, indicating that events are not produced by fixed natures but by a willful agent.

Key Terms
إسكار
شبع
ري
نماء
زرع
سقي
تسميد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natural causes like water and food always produce their effects (e.g., growth, satiety) regardless of quantity or context.
Correction: Baqillani points out that overwatering kills plants and overeating causes pain, showing that effects depend on conditions set by a wise Agent, not fixed natures.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani use as empirical evidence against the theory of natures regarding plant growth?
A) Plants grow only in spring
B) Overwatering kills the plant
C) Fertilizer always helps growth
D) Sun is necessary for growth

Key Takeaway
The inconsistency of natural effects under varying conditions proves that events are not caused by inherent natures but by a willful and wise Agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often attribute your successes to hard work and your failures to bad luck. But this passage teaches that natural causes are not sufficient—all outcomes are directly from Allah. Do you truly believe that your last achievement was from Him alone, or do you still feel a sense of personal pride? How would your gratitude change if you internalized this?
?
When you overindulge in food or drink, do you blame the 'nature' of the food for your pain, or do you see it as a direct sign from Allah that you have transgressed His limits? What would it mean for your self-control to recognize that the harm comes from His will, not from the food itself?
?
The plant's growth stops and dies beyond a point—your life also has limits set by Allah. Are you content with the boundaries He has placed for you, or do you secretly wish for more, thinking you know better than the All-Wise? What would it take for you to submit completely to His decree?

Causality and Created Things

Refuting Natural Causation through Observed Irregularity
Baqillani disproves the theory of natures using examples of regular causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the theory of natural causation by pointing out the lack of uniformity in cause-effect relationships, thereby proving that the true cause is a voluntary agent.

Core Idea
If effects were from natures, they would be uniform; exceptions prove a voluntary Creator.

Baqillani presents a powerful argument against those who claim that natural properties (ṭabā'iʿ) are the real causes behind observed effects. He uses everyday examples to show that if these properties were inherent, their effects would occur consistently, but we see clear exceptions. This leads him to conclude that the true cause is Allah's will.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Read the Arabic passage together with the translation. Notice Baqillani's opening claim: every effect must have a cause, and effects repeat with causes.
This is a foundational premise accepted even by opponents.
2
Identify the examples
Baqillani provides three examples: knowledge/will, pain from hitting, and plant growth. For each, he shows that the effect does not always follow the supposed cause.
Focus on how he uses counterexamples: excessive watering kills plants, overeating does not produce continued satiety.
3
Understand the logical inference
If these effects were truly from a nature inherent in the object, they would occur uniformly whenever the object is present. The fact that they do not shows that the cause is not a nature but a voluntary agent who creates the effect when He wills.
This is the core of Baqillani's refutation.
4
Connect to the broader refutation
Baqillani ends by referencing an earlier proof that accidents cannot act, further undermining the claim that natures (which are accidents) are causes.
This solidifies the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل أيضاً على إبطال قولهم بفعل الطبائع علمنا بوجوب وجود كل معلول بعلة، كلما وجدت وتكررت وكلما وجد مثلها، ووجوب كثرة المسببات عند كثرة أسبابها على قول من أثبت السبب والمسبب. ألا ترى إلى وجوب كون العالم عالماً بالشيء والمريد مريداً له كلما تكررت له الإرادة والعلم وجد به أمثالها في كل وقت وزمان ولم يجز أن توجد به الحكم في بعض الأماكن والأزمان ولا يوجد الحكم؟ وكذلك يجب عند القائلين بتولد الألم عن الضرب وذهاب الجسم عند الدفعة أن يكثرا عند كثرة أسبابهما ويشتدا عند كثرة الضرب والاعتماد والدفع. وكذلك يجب لو كان الإسكار والشبع والري ونماء الزرع حادثاً عن طبع الشراب والطعام والسقي والتسميد وحمى الشمس لوجب أن تزداد هذه الأمور ما كانت الأجسام محتملة لها عند وجود أمثال ما أوجب ذلك وتناوله فكان يجب أن يزيد الزرع وينمي وإن بلغ حد النهاية في مستقر العادة إذا أديم سقيه وأكثر تسميده وإظهاره للشمس حتى يزيد أبداً وينمي وأن توجب له هذه الأمور الزيادة في غير إبان الزرع وحينه كما توجب ذلك في وقت عادة خروجه وفي علمنا أن السقي والتسميد يعود بتلفه إذا بلغ مقداراً ما وأنه لا يوجب له في ذلك غير حين نمائه دليل على سقوط ما قالوه، وكذلك فلو أن الإنسان أكل وشرب فوق شبعه لم يحدث له أبداً من ذلك الشبع والري ما يحدث عند الحاجة إلى تناول الطعام والشراب بل يصير ضرراً وألماً وإذا كان هذا هكذا وجب بطلان ما قالوه، وفسد أن تكون الطبائع التي في هذه الأشياء بزعمهم موجبةً لهذه الأمور، لا على حد إيجاب العلة للحكم، ولا على سبيل ما تولد عن سبب يوجبه، على مذهب أصحاب التولد. وقد ثبت أيضاً بما قدمناه أنه لا يجوز أن تكون الأعراض فاعلة للأفعال؛ فبطل ما يثبتونه من فعل الطبائع أو إيجابها لهذه الحوادث.
And also indicating the invalidity of their statement of the action of natures is our knowledge of the necessity of the existence of every effect by a cause, whenever it occurs and repeats, and whenever its like occurs, and the necessity of the multiplicity of effects when their causes are many, according to the one who affirms the cause and the effect. Do you not see the necessity of the knower being knowing of the thing and the willing being willing of it whenever will and knowledge are repeated for him, and likes of it exist by him in every time and moment, and it is not permissible that the judgment exists by him in some places and times and not exist the judgment? And likewise it is necessary, according to those who say that pain is generated from hitting and the body's disappearance at the push, that they increase when their causes are many and become intense when the hitting, leaning, and pushing are many. And likewise, if intoxication, satiety, thirst-quenching, and the growth of plants were occurring from the nature of the drink, food, watering, fertilizing, and the sun's heat, it would be necessary that these things increase as long as bodies can bear them when likes of what caused that and their consumption exist. Then it would be necessary that the plant increases and grows even if it reached the utmost limit in normal custom if its watering is continued, its fertilizing and exposure to the sun are increased, so that it increases perpetually and grows, and that these things cause increase in it outside the season and time of the plant as they cause that in the usual time of its emergence. And our knowledge that watering and fertilizing lead to its destruction if it reaches a certain amount, and that they do not cause it at other than its time of growth, is evidence for the failure of what they said. And likewise, if a person ate and drank beyond his fill, there would never occur from that the satiety and thirst-quenching that occur when there is need to consume food and drink, but rather it becomes harm and pain. And if this is so, the invalidity of what they said is necessary, and it is false that the natures that are in these things, according to their claim, are causing these things, neither in the manner of a cause's causing of the judgment, nor in the manner of what is generated from a cause that necessitates it, according to the school of the supporters of generation. And it has also been established by what we have presented that it is not permissible for accidents to be doers of actions; so what they affirm of the action of natures or their causing of these events is invalid.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the theory of natures (ṭabā'iʿ) as causes of observed effects. Baqillani begins by stating that we know by necessity that every effect has a cause, and that when causes are repeated, effects are repeated accordingly. This is a premise accepted by those who affirm cause and effect. He then gives several examples: the necessary connection between knowledge/will and the act of knowing/willing; the generation of pain from hitting; the production of intoxication, satiety, growth of plants from their supposed natural sources. In each case, if these effects were due to a nature inherent in the object, they would occur whenever the object is present and conditions are met, and they would increase as the object or its intensity increases. But we observe the opposite: excessive watering kills plants, overeating causes discomfort instead of continued satiety, etc. Therefore, the effects are not due to a nature but to a voluntary agent who creates them according to His will. Baqillani then concludes that the natures claimed by opponents are not causes in the true sense, nor are they generated causes as some (like the Mu'tazila) hold. He further states that it has been proven earlier that accidents cannot act, so the claim of natures (which are accidents) acting is false.

علة
سبب
تولد
أعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Observed regularity proves natural causation inherent in objects.
Correction: Baqillani argues that exceptions (like overwatering killing plants) show that the cause is not a nature but a voluntary Creator who chooses to create effects.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence Baqillani uses to refute the theory of natures?
A) Effects do not always follow their assumed causes, as seen in exceptions.
B) Causes must be eternal and the world is temporal.
C) Natural objects are composed of atoms and accidents.
D) The senses cannot perceive causality directly.

Key Takeaway
Observed regularity is not due to inherent natures but to the constant will of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your day and expect certain outcomes based on the 'laws of nature,' do you genuinely feel that every breath, every heartbeat, every result is directly willed by Allah at that very moment, or do you mentally assign causality to natural processes? What would it take to shift that feeling to a constant awareness of Allah’s direct involvement? Will you train your heart to see His hand in every detail, starting with the next event you notice?
?
Consider a time when you experienced something unexpected—a recovery from illness, a sudden rain, or even a failure despite correct preparation. Did that moment challenge your assumption of natural causation? Did you thank Allah directly for breaking the pattern or did you call it a mere 'exception'? This lesson forces you to admit that all outcomes—good or bad—are not from natures but from Allah’s will. Will you now consciously praise Him for every single blessing as a direct gift rather than a natural result?
?
Baqillani points out that overeating leads to pain, not increased satiety. How often do you forget this and still pursue worldly pleasures thinking they will bring lasting satisfaction? Has this lesson exposed a deep-seated belief in the 'nature' of things to bring you fulfillment? What if you truly internalized that no created thing can benefit or harm except by Allah’s direct command—would your cravings, worries, and ambitions change? What concrete step can you take today to remind yourself of this truth in a moment of desire or fear?

Refutation of Sense-Knowledge of Natural Causation
Baqillani refutes the claim that burning is known by sense to be from fire.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Imam al-Baqillani, we cannot know through sense perception that fire burns or wine intoxicates, and why the causal link requires rational investigation.

Core Idea
We never see causality; we only see change. The cause is known by reason, not by the senses.

Imam al-Baqillani confronts a common claim: that we directly sense fire burning or wine intoxicating. He calls this 'great ignorance' and shows that what we actually perceive is only the change in the body, not the cause. This lesson unpacks his argument and the different opinions about causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the strong opening dismissal and the list of opinions.
The Arabic text ends abruptly; the argument is incomplete, but the main points are clear.
2
Identify Baqillani's central claim
Find where Baqillani states his main argument: what we sense is only the change, not the cause. Notice how he distinguishes between observation and inference.
He says the knowledge of the cause is 'not observed but perceived by precise examination and research.'
3
Examine the four opinions on causation
Baqillani lists four different explanations for the cause of burning/intoxication. List each one and notice how they disagree. Ask yourself: if causation were sensed, why would there be such disagreement?
The opinions range from God's action to nature as an accident.
4
Reflect on the refutation's force
Consider Baqillani's final point: if one person could claim necessity for their view, another could claim the opposite. This shows the claim is not necessary or sensed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما ما يهذون به كثيراً من أنهم يعلمون حساً واضطراراً أن الإحراق والإسكار الحادثين واقعان عن حرارة النار وشدة الشراب فإنه جهل عظيم وذلك أن الذي نشاهده ونحسه إنما هو تغير حال الجسم عند تناول الشراب ومجاورة النار وكونه سكراناً ومحترقاً ومتغيراً عما كان عليه فقط؛ فأما العلم بأن هذه الحالة الحادثة المتجددة من فعل من هي، فإنه غير مشاهَد بل مدرك بدقيق الفحص والبحث فمن قائل: إنه من قديم مخترع قادر وهو الحق الذي نذهب إليه؛ ومن قائل يقول: إنه من فعل الإنسان الذي جاور النار وتناول الشراب ومتولد عن فعله الذي هو سبب الإحراق والإسكار؛ ومن قائل يقول إنه فعل الطبع في الجسم ولا أدري أهو نفس الجسم المطبوع أم معنى فيه؛ ومن قائل يقول: إن الطبع عرض من الأعراض. فكيف يدرك حقيقة ما قد اختلف فيه هذا الضرب من الاختلاف بالمشاهدة ودرك الحواس؟ ولو جاز لزاعم أن يزعم أنه يعلم صحة فعل الطبع في الجسم لما حدث من هذه الأمور اضطراراً لجاز لنا أن ندعي أنا نعلم كذب مدعي ذلك اضطراراً وأنه هو مضطر إلى ذلك؛ وهذا مما لا
As for what they often babble, that they know by sense and necessity that the burning and intoxication that occur are from the heat of fire and the potency of wine — it is great ignorance. And that is because what we witness and sense is only the change of the state of the body upon consuming the wine and approaching the fire, and its being drunk, burnt, and changed from what it was, only. As for the knowledge that this occurring, renewed state is from the action of whom it is, it is not observed but perceived by precise examination and research. For there are those who say: it is from an eternal, inventive, powerful [being] — and that is the truth which we hold. And there are those who say: it is from the action of the human who approached the fire and consumed the wine, and generated from his action which is the cause of burning and intoxication. And there are those who say: it is the action of the nature in the body — and I do not know whether it is the very body that is endowed with nature or a meaning in it. And there are those who say: the nature is an accident among accidents. So how can the reality of what has been disputed in this manner of disagreement be perceived by observation and the grasp of the senses? And if it were permissible for a claimant to claim that he knows the validity of the action of nature in the body regarding what occurs of these things by necessity, it would be permissible for us to claim that we know the falsity of the claimant of that by necessity, and that he is compelled to that. And this is something that does not...
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that we directly sense that fire burns or that wine intoxicates. Baqillani calls such a claim 'great ignorance'. He explains that what we actually sense is only the change in the body (becoming drunk or burnt), not the cause of that change. Identifying the cause requires rational inquiry, and there are multiple opinions: the Ashʿarī view (attribution to God), the Muʿtazilī view (generated from human action), the naturalist view (action of a nature in the body), and even a view that nature is an accident. Since there is such disagreement, one cannot claim to know the cause by sense perception. Baqillani also points out that if someone could claim by necessity that nature is the cause, one could just as well claim by necessity that they are wrong, showing the claim is not necessary. The argument is cut off at the end of the page.

Key Terms
حس
اضطرار
فعل الطبع
مشاهدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People often think they see causality (e.g., fire burning), but they only see succession; the causal link is inferred, not sensed.
Correction: Baqillani argues that we only perceive the change in the body, not the cause. The cause is known through rational inquiry, not sense.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what do we actually perceive when we see fire burning wood?
A) The fire creating the burning.
B) Only the change in the wood, not the cause.
C) Both the fire and the burning by a natural necessity.
D) An accident inhering in the fire.

Key Takeaway
Sense perception alone cannot establish causation; the cause is known through rational inquiry and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you were absolutely certain that something caused a specific outcome in your life — e.g., a person's action caused you pain, or a change in circumstances caused your happiness. Based on this lesson, what evidence did you actually have from your senses, and what did you infer? How willing are you to re-examine that certainty and ask: 'Did I truly see the cause, or only the change?'
?
Baqillani shows that even rational people disagree about causes. When you hold a strong opinion about a religious or worldly matter, do you assume that your view is 'obvious' or 'common sense'? After reading this passage, will you commit to probing your own assumptions and distinguishing what you actually observe from what you infer?

Causality and Created Things
beginner

The Invalidity of Natural Properties as Causes
Baqillani concludes that natural properties cannot cause events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that natural properties cannot cause events, based on the principle that accidents cannot be agents.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot act; natural properties are accidents, so they cannot cause anything.

This lesson presents Baqillani's concluding argument against the naturalist view that properties in things have inherent causal power. Building on earlier proofs that accidents cannot be agents of actions, he shows that the claim of natural properties causing events is invalid. Let's examine how he connects these ideas.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how Baqillani first describes the naturalists' claim and then invokes a principle.
The passage is a conclusion, so it references earlier arguments.
2
Identify the two key parts
First, Baqillani says the naturalists' claim is not like true causation or generation. Second, he states the established principle that accidents cannot be agents.
The word 'ثابت' indicates the principle is already proven.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since natural properties are accidents, they cannot act. Therefore, their claim to cause events is invalid (بطل).
This is a direct application of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التي في هذه الأشياء بزعمهم موجبةً لهذه الأمور، لا على حد إيجاب العلة للحكم، ولا على سبيل ما تولد عن سبب يوجبه، على مذهب أصحاب التولد . وقد ثبت أيضاً بما قدمناه أنه لا يجوز أن تكون الأعراض فاعلة للأفعال ؛ فبطل ما يثبتونه من فعل الطبائع أو إيجابها لهذه الحوادث .
...which they claim are necessitating these things, not in the manner of a cause necessitating its effect, nor in the manner of that which is generated from a cause that necessitates it, according to the school of those who believe in generation. And it has also been established by what we have presented that it is not permissible for accidents to be agents of actions; so what they affirm of the action of natural properties or their necessitation of these events is invalidated.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion drawn from previous arguments. It begins by describing the claim of the naturalists: they assert that properties in things necessitate their effects, but not in the manner of a true cause (علة) necessitating its effect, nor in the manner of generated effects (تولد) as held by the Mu'tazila. Then the author invokes a previously established principle: accidents (أعراض) cannot be agents of actions. Since natural properties are accidents (according to Ash'ari ontology), they cannot act. Therefore, the claim that natural properties cause events is invalid.

Key Terms
طبائع
أعراض
تولد
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ash'aris deny all causality entirely.
Correction: Baqillani focuses on the impossibility of accidents acting as agents; he is not denying that Allah creates events in a customary sequence.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the underlying principle that invalidates the claim of natural properties causing events?
A) Accidents cannot be agents of actions.
B) Fire does not burn by nature.
C) The Mu'tazila are wrong about generation.
D) Knowledge of causation is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Natural properties cannot cause events because they are accidents, and accidents cannot be agents of actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your own skills, health, or circumstances as independent causes of success or failure, forgetting that they are accidents that cannot act? How would your daily reliance on Allah change if you truly internalized that nothing in creation can cause anything without His will?
?
When you face a setback, do you blame a 'natural cause' (e.g., bad luck, someone's behavior, a system) as if it had power over you? How does Baqillani's refutation of natural properties as agents challenge you to turn immediately to Allah in that moment?
?
You have just learned that accidents cannot act. Yet you still act as if your own decisions and efforts are the real causes of your outcomes. What would it cost you to live today with the conviction that only Allah brings about every effect—and to act accordingly?

Refuting Necessity of Causal Knowledge in Sensation
Baqillani refutes claim that burning and intoxication are known by necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that the cause of burning and intoxication cannot be known through sense experience alone.

Core Idea
Sense perception shows change, not its cause.

This passage tackles a fundamental misconception about causality. Many assume that when we see fire burn, we directly know that the fire causes the burning. Baqillani dismantles this assumption by clarifying what we actually observe versus what requires deeper investigation. He then presents four competing explanations for the observed change, showing that the cause is not immediately apparent.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then read the literal translation. Notice the strong opening dismissal and the shift to analysis.
The Arabic uses 'يهذون' (babbling) to set a confrontational tone.
2
Distinguish observation from inference
Identify Baqillani's key move: he separates what is directly sensed (the change) from what is known by investigation (the cause). This is the core of his refutation.
3
List the four competing views
Find and list the four different opinions about the cause of the change. Notice how each view attributes causality to a different agent or principle.
The views are: (1) from God, (2) from human act via generation, (3) from a natural property in the body, (4) from the property as an accident.
4
Understand the conclusion
Baqillani concludes that since there is such disagreement about the true cause, it cannot be known by simple observation. Reflect on why this refutes the claim of necessity.
This passage is a foundational argument for Ash'ari occasionalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما ما يهذون به كثيراً من أنهم يعلمون حِسّاً واضطراراً أن الإحراق والإسكار الحادثين واقعان عن حرارة النار وشدة الشراب فإنه جهل عظيم وذلك أن الذي نشاهده ونحسه إنما هو تغير حال الجسم عند تناول الشراب ومجاورة النار وكونه سكران ومحترقاً ومتغيراً عما كان عليه فقط ; فأما العلم بأن هذه الحالة الحادثة المتجددة من فِعْلِ مَنْ هِيَ، فإنه غير مشاهَدٍ بل مُدْرَك بدقيق الفحص والبحث فمن قائل: إنه من قديم مخترع قادر وهو الحق الذي نذهب إليه ; ومن قائل يقول : إنه من فعل الإنسان الذي جاور النار وتناول الشرابَ ومتولّد عن فعله الذي هو سبب الإحراق والإسكار; ومن قائل يقول إنه فعل الطبع في الجسم ولا أدري أهو نفس الجسم المطبوع أم معنى فيه ; ومن قائل يقول : إن الطبع عرض من الأعراض. فكيف يُدْرَك حقيقةُ ما قد اختلف فيه هذا الضرب من الاختلاف بالمشاهدة ودَرْكِ الحواس؟
As for what they frequently babble about, that they know by sense and necessity that the burning and intoxication that occur are brought about by the heat of fire and the strength of wine, this is great ignorance. For what we witness and sense is only the change of the body's state upon consuming the drink and proximity to fire, and its being drunk and burnt and changed from what it was, nothing more. As for knowledge of this new occurring state, from whose act it is, it is not observed but rather known by careful examination and investigation. Some say it is from an eternal, invented, powerful being – that is the truth which we hold. Some say it is from the act of the human who was near the fire and consumed the drink, generated from his act which is the cause of burning and intoxication. Some say it is the action of the natural property in the body, and I do not know whether it is the body itself having the property or a meaning in it. Some say that the natural property is an accident among accidents. So how can the reality of what is disputed with this kind of disagreement be known by observation and sense perception?
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the empiricist argument. The author first dismisses the claim as 'great ignorance'. He explains that sensory observation only reveals the change in the body, not the cause. To know the cause requires investigation, and there is great disagreement: the correct view (Ash'ari) attributes it to God; some attribute it to human action via generation; some to a natural property in the body; others say the property is an accident. Given this disagreement, how can one claim to know the cause by simple observation? Thus, the claim of necessity is false.

Key Terms
علم ضروري
مشاهدة
حواس
إحراق
إسكار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: We directly perceive that fire causes burning and wine causes intoxication.
Correction: We only perceive the change in the body; the cause is not directly sensed but requires rational investigation.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani claim is the only thing observed in the case of fire and wine?
A) The heat of fire and strength of wine.
B) The change in the body's state.
C) The act of God.
D) The natural property.

Key Takeaway
The cause of observed events is not directly sensed; it requires rational investigation, as shown by the multiplicity of opinions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a good outcome—like passing an exam or recovering from illness—do you immediately attribute it to your own effort or to natural causes, without acknowledging that the true cause is Allah? How would your gratitude change if you truly believed that every effect is directly from Him?
?
This passage shows that even intelligent people disagree on the cause of simple events like burning. Are you quick to assume you know the causes of events in your life? Will you commit to pausing and remembering that the real cause may be beyond your observation, and that careful reflection is needed?
?
The author calls the opposing view 'great ignorance.' Is there an area in your life where you claim certainty based on what you've seen, while ignoring the deeper investigation required? What would it cost you to humbly admit that you don't know the ultimate cause, and to seek it from the One who does?

Baqillani's Refutation of Necessary Natural Causation
Baqillani argues that if natural causation were necessary, its denial would also be necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses a symmetry argument and an analogy to refute the claim that knowledge of natural causation is necessary.

Core Idea
If natural causation were necessary, its denial would also be necessary — an absurd contradiction.

Baqillani here tackles a central claim of naturalists: that we know natural causation by necessity. He responds with a clever symmetry argument — if the naturalist can claim necessary knowledge, we can claim necessary knowledge of its falsity. Then he strengthens his case by comparing natural causation to truly undeniable truths, showing that the widespread denial of natural causation proves it is not necessary at all.

Guided Path
1
Read Baqillani's argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Baqillani structures his refutation in two parts: a direct symmetry argument and an analogy with undeniable necessary truths.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا حيلة لهم في الخلاص منه' — he claims no escape.
2
Analyze the symmetry argument
Identify the conditional: if the naturalist can claim necessary knowledge of causation, then we can claim necessary knowledge that he is false. This creates a contradiction, refuting the original claim.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the analogy with necessary truths
Baqillani gives examples of things truly known by necessity: the sky above us, the earth below us, hearing the speaker. These cannot be denied by any rational community. But natural causation is widely denied, so it cannot be a necessary truth.
4
Draw the conclusion
The widespread denial of natural causation throughout history proves that it is not necessary knowledge. The claimant's ignorance is exposed by the fact that most people reject his claim.
Baqillani uses empirical observation of disagreement as evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو جاز لزاعم أن يزعم أنه يعلم صحة فعل الطبع في الجسم لما حدث من هذه الأمور اضطراراً لجاز لنا أن ندعي أنا نعلم كذب مُدَّعي ذلك اضطراراً وأنه هو مضطرّ إلى ذلك ; وهذا مما لا حيلة لهم في الخلاص منه، وقد ثبت أن ما يُعلم بالضرورة ودرك الحواس لا يجوز أن يجتمع على جَحْدِه وإنكاره قوم بهم تثبت الحجة وينقطع العذرُ كما لا يجوز مثل ذلك في جحد وجودنا السماء فوقنا والأرض تحتنا وسماع كلام السائل لنا في هذا الباب; وفي جحد أكثر العالم والأمم لفعل الطباع والعلم بهذه الطبائع أصلاً دليل على جهل مدعي هذه الدعوى .
And if it were permissible for a claimant to claim that he knows the validity of the action of the natural property in the body regarding what occurs from these things by necessity, then it would be permissible for us to claim that we know the falsity of that claimant by necessity and that he is compelled to that. And there is no escape for them from this. And it has been established that what is known by necessity and sense perception cannot have a people by whom proof is established and excuse is cut off unite in denying it and rejecting it, just as such is not permissible regarding the denial of the existence of the sky above us and the earth beneath us and hearing the speech of the interlocutor to us in this chapter. And the denial of most of the world and nations of the action of natural properties and knowledge of these properties at all is evidence of the ignorance of the claimant of this claim.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage employs a reductio ad absurdum and an analogy. If the naturalist could claim necessary knowledge of natural causation, then we could equally claim necessary knowledge that he is false, which would create a contradiction. Moreover, truly necessary knowledge (like the sky being overhead) is such that no rational community can deny it. But we find that many people deny natural causation, proving it is not necessary. The widespread denial itself is evidence of the claimant's ignorance.

Key Terms
اضطرار
جحد
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary knowledge means that every single individual must accept it.
Correction: Baqillani shows that truly necessary knowledge cannot be denied by any rational community that serves as a proof, but it does not require universal acceptance by every individual; however, widespread denial by rational communities indicates it is not necessary.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani use as an example of something that is truly known by necessity?
A) The heat of fire.
B) The existence of the sky above us.
C) The action of natural properties.
D) The generation of effects.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes necessary knowledge of natural causation by showing it leads to contradiction and is denied by many rational people.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you have ever been absolutely certain of something, only to find that intelligent people disagree, how does Baqillani's argument force you to re-evaluate the grounds of that certainty? What is one belief you hold that you must now test by this standard?
?
Consider a moment you attributed an outcome to a 'natural cause' without consciously acknowledging Allah's immediate control. How does this passage expose that as a failure of tawhid? What habit can you adopt to remind yourself that every effect is Allah's direct act?
?
Baqillani says that what is known by necessity cannot be denied by any sound-minded person. Is there any claim about religious truth that you think is so obvious that its denial is inexcusable? How would you defend that claim using Baqillani's method without falling into the same error he refutes here?

Arguments Against Necessary Knowledge of Causation
Baqillani cites consensus and analogy to refute necessary knowledge of causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that knowledge of causation is not necessary but rational, and his use of consensus and analogy to refute claims of necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowing the cause of an event is not a necessary perception but a rational inference.

In this passage, Baqillani challenges the idea that we can know the cause of events through direct necessity. He points to widespread disagreement and consensus among theologians that origination from non-existence is not known necessarily. Then he draws a clever analogy with Mu'tazili claims of 'generation' to show the absurdity of claiming necessary knowledge of causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the flow. Notice the rhetorical questions and the shift to the analogy.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage contains two main arguments: first, the denial of necessary knowledge of specific events and the consensus against necessary knowledge of origination; second, an analogy with Mu'tazili generation.
3
Understand the consensus claim
Baqillani asserts that all theologians agree that the occurrence of a thing from non-existence is not known by necessity. This undermines the claim of necessary knowledge of causation.
4
Analyze the analogy
Baqillani compares the claim of necessary knowledge of causation to a Mu'tazili claim of necessary knowledge of generated effects, implying both are equally baseless.
The text is cut off, but the implication is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى أن كثيراً من المتكلمين ينكر العلم بوجود حوادث هي إحراق وإسكار من جهة الاضطرار فكيف يعلم حدوثها من مُحْدِث بعينه وعن شيء بعينه اضطراراً؟ وكثير من الناس يجهلون وجود هذه الأعراض وأعيانها فكيف يُضطرون مع ذلك إلى العلم بفاعلها؟ وعلى أن سائر المتكلمين وأهل التحصيل قد أطبقوا على أن حدوث الشيء وكونه عن عدم لا يُعلم اضطراراً ; فكيف يُعلم مِمَّن حدث؟ وعن أي شيء حدث اضطراراً؟ والعلم بحدث الشيء وما حدث عنه فرغ للعلم بأنه مُحْدَث; فإذا لم يَعْلَمُه مُحْدَثاً، اضطراراً، ولم يشاهده معدومـاً قبل وجوده وموجـوداً بعد عـدمـه، فكيف يُضطر إلى العلم بمُحْدِثه، لولا الغفلة والذهاب عن التحصيل؟. ويُقال لهم في هذا أيضاً لو قال لكم قـائـل من المعتزلة القائلين بالتولد: إنني أعلم حدوث الألم وذهاب السهم والحجر متولدين عن الرمي والدفع والاعتماد وكذلك الكسر والقطع وتأليف الأجسام عند حركات البنائين واعتمادهم ; وإنني أشاهده وأحسه اضطراراً، هل كان في دعواه ذلك إلا بمنزلتكم في إدعاء فعل الطباع ؟ لأنه يقول : وَجَدْتُ هذه الأمور
And moreover, many theologians deny knowledge of the existence of events that are burning and intoxication by way of necessity, so how can one know their occurrence from a specific originator and from a specific thing by necessity? And many people are ignorant of the existence of these accidents and their essences, so how are they compelled, despite that, to knowledge of their agent? And moreover, all theologians and people of accomplishment have agreed that the occurrence of a thing and its being from non-existence is not known by necessity. So how can one know from whom it occurred? And from which thing it occurred by necessity? And knowledge of the occurrence of the thing and from what it occurred is a branch of knowledge that it is originated. So if one does not know it as originated by necessity, and does not observe it as non-existent before its existence and existent after its non-existence, how can one be compelled to knowledge of its originator, were it not for heedlessness and departure from investigation? And it is said to them in this also: if a speaker from the Mu'tazila who believe in generation said to you: 'I know the occurrence of pain and the going of the arrow and stone as generated from shooting and pushing and reliance, and similarly breaking, cutting, and composition of bodies at the movements of builders and their reliance; and I witness it and sense it by necessity,' would his claim be in any station other than your station in claiming the action of natural properties? Because he says: 'I have found these things...'
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage combines multiple arguments. First, many theologians deny that burning and intoxication are known necessarily, so the claim of necessary knowledge of their cause is unfounded. Second, there is consensus that the origination of something from non-existence is not known necessarily; thus, knowledge of the cause is derivative and not necessary. Third, the author presents an analogy: if a Mu'tazili claimed necessary knowledge of generated effects (e.g., pain from shooting), would that be any different from the naturalist's claim? The text ends with the Mu'tazili's assertion, but the implication is that both claims are equally baseless.

Key Terms
محدث
حدوث
تولد
بنائين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Baqillani denies any knowledge of causation, but he only denies that it is necessary and self-evident.
Correction: Baqillani's argument is that knowledge of causation is rational and acquired, not given by sense perception or necessity.

Checkpoint
What is the consensus among theologians according to Baqillani?
A) Burning is caused by fire.
B) The occurrence of a thing from non-existence is not known by necessity.
C) Accidents can act.
D) Generation is true.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani argues that knowledge of causation is not necessary but rational, using consensus and analogy to refute claims of necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Why Nature's Actions Cannot Be Known Necessarily
Baqillani argues that actions attributed to nature cannot be known necessarily.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that knowledge of nature's actions (e.g., burning, intoxication) cannot be necessary (ḍarūrī) and how this refutes the claim that causality is known instinctively.

Core Idea
If something were known necessarily, everyone would agree—but they don't.

Baqillani tackles a key claim of natural causality: that burning and intoxication are known necessarily from natures. He shows this cannot be true because necessary knowledge is universal and undeniable, yet many people are ignorant of these causes. He also appeals to scholarly consensus that coming-into-existence itself is not known necessarily, so its cause cannot be either.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let Baqillani's argument unfold naturally.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main claim being refuted
Baqillani refutes those who claim that actions like burning and intoxication are known necessarily (ḍarūran) to be caused by natures (ṭabā'i').
This is a specific claim about necessary knowledge of causality.
3
Follow Baqillani's first argument: necessary knowledge is universal
He argues that what is known necessarily cannot be denied by a community that establishes proof. Since many deny or are ignorant of these causal relations, they cannot be necessary.
Key point: disagreement disproves necessity.
4
Notice the consensus argument
Baqillani states that most mutakallimūn agree that coming-into-existence (ḥudūth) itself is not known necessarily, so knowledge of the specific agent cannot be necessary either.
This strengthens his case by appealing to scholarly agreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حيلة لهم في الخلاص منه، وقد ثبت أن ما يُعلم بالضرورة ودرك الحواس لا يجوز أن يجتمع على جَحْدِه وإنكاره قوم بهم تثبت الحجة وينقطع العذرُ كما لا يجوز مثل ذلك في جحد وجودنا السماء فوقنا والأرض تحتنا وسماع كلام السائل لنا في هذا الباب؛ وفي جحد أكثر العالم والأمم لفعل الطباع والعلم بهذه الطبائع أصلاً دليل على جهل مدعي هذه الدعوى . وعلى أن كثيراً من المتكلمين ينكر العلم بوجود حوادث هي إحراق وإسكار من جهة الاضطرار فكيف يعلم حدوثها من مُحْدِث بعينه وعن شيء بعينه اضطراراً؟ وكثير من الناس يجهلون وجود هذه الأعراض وأعيانها فكيف يُضطرون مع ذلك إلى العلم بفاعلها؟ وعلى أن سائر المتكلمين وأهل التحصيل قد أطبقوا على أن حدوث الشيء وكونه عن عدم لا يُعلم اضطراراً ; فكيف يُعلم مِمَّن حدث؟ وعن أي شيء حدث اضطراراً؟ والعلم بحدث الشيء وما حدث عنه فرغ للعلم بأنه مُحْدَث; فإذا لم يَعْلَمُه مُحْدَثاً، اضطراراً، ولم يشاهده معدومـاً قبل وجوده وموجـوداً بعد عـدمـه، فكيف يُضطر إلى العلم بمُحْدِثه، لولا الغفلة والذهاب عن التحصيل؟
A trick for them to escape from it. It has been established that what is known necessarily and by perception of the senses is not permissible for a people by whom the proof is established and the excuse is cut off to gather upon denying and rejecting it, just as the like of that is not permissible in denying our existence: the sky above us and the earth beneath us and hearing the speech of the questioner to us in this chapter. And in the denial of most of the world and nations of the action of natures (tabā'i'), and knowledge of these natures at all, is evidence of the ignorance of the claimant of this claim. And also that many of the mutakallimūn deny knowledge of the existence of events that are burning and intoxication from the aspect of necessity; so how can its coming into existence be known from a specific producer (muḥdith) and from a specific thing necessarily? And many people are ignorant of the existence of these accidents and their essences; so how can they be compelled, despite that, to knowledge of their agent? And also that the rest of the mutakallimūn and people of verification have agreed that the coming into existence of a thing and its being from non-existence is not known necessarily; so how can it be known from whom it occurred? And from which thing did it occur necessarily? And knowledge of the occurrence of a thing and what it occurred from is a branch of knowledge that it is produced (muḥdath); so if he does not know it as produced, necessarily, and does not observe it as non-existent before its existence and existent after its non-existence, how can he be compelled to knowledge of its producer (muḥdith), were it not for heedlessness and departure from verification?
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who claim that actions like burning and intoxication are caused necessarily by natures (tabā'i'). Baqillani argues that such knowledge cannot be claimed as necessary (ḍarūrī) because: 1) Necessary knowledge (e.g., sky above, earth below) cannot be denied by a community whose testimony establishes proof; yet nature advocates deny that many people are ignorant of these causes. 2) Many mutakallimūn deny that such events are known necessarily at all, so how can their specific cause be known necessarily? 3) The majority of mutakallimūn agree that coming into existence (ḥudūth) itself is not known necessarily; thus, knowledge of the agent is also not necessary. The passage concludes that claiming necessary knowledge of a specific producer is due to heedlessness.

مُحْدَث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that we know necessarily (ḍarūran) that fire burns by nature, without any inference.
Correction: Baqillani argues that this cannot be necessary knowledge because many people are ignorant of or deny such causal relations; necessary knowledge is universal and undeniable.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani reject the claim that actions like burning and intoxication are known necessarily from natures?

Key Takeaway
Baqillani demonstrates that claims of necessary causal knowledge are invalid because such knowledge would be universally held, which it is not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does Baqillani's argument challenge that assumption, and what does that mean for how you understand God's role in every event?
?
If most people—including many scholars—disagree on whether we know causes necessarily, can you really claim that your own causal judgments are beyond doubt? Are you content to rely on heedless habit rather than careful verification?
?
This lesson argues that ignorance of the true producer (God) often comes from heedlessness. Where in your daily life do you most naturally see the world as self-causing, forgetting the Creator? What would it take to turn every event into a reminder of Him?
